The Ten Categories
intermediate
8 min
Refuting Objections on Passion and Motion: Process vs. State
Author refutes two objections against the categories of passion and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that passion is distinct from quality and that motion is not a genus of action and passion, using the distinction between stable and non-stable states.

Core Idea
Passion is a process, not a stable quality; motion is not an action of the agent.

After defining the ten categories, the author defends the inclusion of passion (an yanfaʿil) and action (an yafʿal) as distinct categories against two objections. The first objection claims passion is merely quality; the second claims motion is a genus covering both action and passion. The author refutes these using distinctions from natural science (al-ʿilm al-ṭabīʿī). This lesson dissects these refutations to sharpen your ability to evaluate ontological distinctions.

Guided Path
1
State the first objection and its premise
The opponent says: 'Passion is nothing but quality (kayfiyya). For example, heating (tasakhkhun) is identical to heat (sukhūna).' Identify the implicit premise: if passion and quality were different, their instances would differ; but heating and heat seem the same.
Heating is the act of becoming hot; heat is the resulting state.
2
Find the author's rebuttal
The author distinguishes heating as a process (سلوك إلى السخونة بهيئة غير قارة) and heat as a stable state (هيئة قارة). Since they differ in stability, they are ontologically distinct. Therefore, passion (which includes processes) cannot be reduced to quality.
The key is the contrast between 'ghayr qārra' (non-stable) and 'qārra' (stable).
3
Analyze the second objection and its refutation
The second objector claims motion is a genus that encompasses both action and passion. The author counters that motion is not attributable to the agent (الفاعل) and that motion itself is not an action (تفعل), as proven in natural science.
In natural philosophy, motion is a quality or process in the movable, not an action of the mover.
4
Reconstruct the logical structure
Both refutations are conditional arguments: If X, then Y. But Y is false, so X is false. For the first: If passion were quality, then heating would be heat. But heating is not heat (process vs state). Therefore passion is not quality. For the second: If motion were a genus of action and passion, then motion would be an action of the agent. But motion is not an action of the agent. Therefore motion is not a genus of action and passion.
The author appeals to established natural science for the minor premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمفهوم عند الجمهور أنها عشرة؛ إذ الذي يكون منها إما أن يكون قائما بذاته وهو الجوهر، أو لا يكون قائما بذاته بل بالغير وهو العرض والعرض لا يخلو من أن يحصل من صدقه صدق القسمة وهو الكمية، أو لا يحصل، وإنه لا يخلو من أن لا يحصل من صدقه صدق النسبة وهو الكيفية، أو يحصل وهو النسبة. والنسبة إما أن تكون مكررة وهي الإضافة أو لا تكون. وإنها إما من جهة المكان وهي الأين، أو الزمان وهي المتى، أو الإحاطة وهي الجُدة، أو الهيئة الحاصلة للجسم وهي الوضع أو الفاعلية وهي أن يفعل، أو القابلية وهي أن ينفعل. وأما قول من قال إن الانفعال هو الكيفية ليس إلا؛ إذ التسخن ليس غير السخونة بل هو عينها» فذلك باطل؛ إذ التسخن وهو السلوك إلى السخونة بهيئة غير قارة والسخونة هيئة قارة. وكذلك قول من يقول إن الحركة جنس لأن يفعل وأن ينفعل»؛ إذ هو باطل أيضًا لما أن الحركة غير موصوف بها الفاعل وإنها ليست تفعل أيضًا على ما عرف في العلم الطبيعي.
And the concept according to the majority is that they are ten; because that which exists is either self-subsistent and that is substance, or not self-subsistent but by another and that is accident. And accident is not free from either that from its truth the truth of division obtains and that is quantity, or it does not obtain. And it is not free from either that from its truth the truth of relation does not obtain and that is quality, or it obtains and that is relation. And relation is either repeated and that is relation (addition), or not. And it is either with respect to place and that is 'ayn, or time and that is matā, or surrounding and that is possession, or the disposition acquired for the body and that is position, or agency and that is to do, or passivity and that is to be acted upon. As for the statement of one who said that passion is nothing but quality, because heating is nothing other than heat but is it itself, that is false; because heating is the progression towards heat in a non-stable state and heat is a stable state. And similarly the statement of one who says that motion is a genus of to do and to be acted upon is also false, because motion is not attributed to the agent and it is not an action either, as is known in natural science.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage defines the ten categories (maqūlāt) as understood by the majority. The author begins by dividing all existents into substance (jawhar) and accident (ʿaraḍ). Accidents are then subdivided: some involve division into parts (quantity: kammiyya), some involve relation (nisba): if relation is not obtained, it is quality; if obtained, it is relation. Relation is further differentiated: if repeated, it is iḍāfa (relation in the specific sense); otherwise, it is place (ayn), time (matā), possession (jidda), position (waḍʿ), action (an yafʿal), or passion (an yanfaʿil). The author then refutes two objections: (1) that passion is merely quality, arguing that heating (tasakhkhun) is a process leading to heat (sukhūna), which is a stable state, so they are distinct; (2) that motion is a genus encompassing action and passion, but motion is not attributable to an agent as an action, as established in natural science.

Key Terms
أن يفعل
أن ينفعل
التسخن
السخونة
الحركة
هيئة غير قارة
هيئة قارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is it false to say that passion (infiʿāl) is nothing but quality?
A) Because passion is a substance.
B) Because heating (tasakhkhun) is a non-stable process while heat (sukhūna) is a stable state.
C) Because the author says all accidents are qualities.
D) Because passion is actually a relation.

Key Takeaway
Passion and action are distinct from quality and from each other; a process differs from the resulting state, and motion is not an action of the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent says: 'Becoming knowledgeable is the same as knowledge itself.' Using the author's distinction between non-stable process and stable state, write a rebuttal showing that 'becoming knowledgeable' (taʿallum) is passion while 'knowledge' (ʿilm) is quality.
?
Identify the fallacy in this claim: 'Since walking is a motion, and motion is a genus of action and passion, therefore walking is both an action and a passion.' Explain why the author would reject the premise about motion.

6 min
Principles Outside the Categories: Unity, Point, Matter, and Form
Author discusses principles beyond categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the arguments for why principles like unity and point are considered outside the ten categories, and evaluate the counterarguments that place them within quantity.

Core Idea
Principles are not in categories because they would then be principles of themselves.

The ten categories are meant to classify all existing things, but some entities seem to transcend classification. In this passage, the author examines principles—unity, point, matter, form—that ground numbers, magnitudes, and substances. Why might they be excluded? And what does that tell us about the limits of the categorical system? This lesson guides you through the logical debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principles listed
Find unity, point, matter, and form in the Arabic. Note their roles: unity as principle of number, point as principle of magnitude, matter and form as principles of substance.
These are called 'mabādi' (principles) in the text.
2
Understand the exclusion argument
The author states that if principles were in a category, they would be principles of themselves, which is absurd. Grasp the circularity: a principle cannot be part of what it grounds.
This is a logical argument for exclusion.
3
Consider the counterargument
Some scholars argue that unity is quantity (number) and the point is quantity (part of a line). See how they bring principles into a specific category.
The counterargument denies that principles are outside categories.
4
Evaluate the logical moves
Does being a principle necessarily exclude being in a category? Reflect on whether the counterargument successfully avoids circularity.
Consider the difference between being a principle and being an instance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في ما يكون في الأوهام من الأمور المباينة لهذه العشرة] وما يكون في الأوهام من الأمور المباينة لهذه العشرة، فذلك كالوحدة التي هي مبدأ العدد والنقطة التي هي مبدأ بوجه ما للمقادير، وأيضًا مثل الهيولى والصورة، وأيضًا مثل الأعدام كالعمى والجهل وما أشبه ذلك. فمنهم من أورد في هذا الباب أمثلة جزئية، نحو الشمال والجنوب والغداء والعشاء؛ فنقول إنه ليس كل وجود الأشياء لا يدخل في المقولات رمز (۱) في أن المقولات عشرة، وليس يجب في بادي الرأي أن يكون لكل ذات موجودة ما يشاركه في الذات موجودًا حتى تكون تلك الذات موجودة، فليس يجب أن يكون لكل شيء نوع مقول على كثيرين بالفعل. ولو كان لكل شيء نوع مقول على كثيرين بالعدد لم يجب أن يكون مع ذلك النوع نوع آخر مشارك له في ماهية مشتركة حتى يكون هناك جنس. فلا يمتنع أن تكون أمور مفردة لا مشارك لها في نوعها، وأنواع هي أنواع بالقياس إلى ما تحتها ولا قياس لها إلى ما فوقها، فإن كانت أشخاص مفردة لا أنواع لها البتة ولا أجناس على الشرط المذكورة، وأنواع لا أجناس لها كذلك لم يكن شيء من ذلك داخلا في مقولة من هذه المقولات. وكان حقًا ما قيل إن المقولات هي هذه العشرة؛ إذ الخارج عنها ليس بمقولة في نفسه ولا في مقولة غيرها، فلو كانت تلك الأشياء خارجة عن المقولات فذلك لا يقدح في كونها عشرة، إلا وأن يصح أن لتلك الأشياء أجناسًا خارجة عن العشرة. وبعد فإن من الأجوبة المشهورة عنها ما نقل عن البعض منهم أن هذه الأشياء خارجة عن العشرة وخصوصًا ما كان منها يجري مجرى المبادئ كالوحدة والنقطة والهيولى والصورة؛ إذ المبادئ لا يدخل في شيء من المقولات لما أنها هي المبادئ لها؛ ولو دخلت فيها لكانت هي مبادئ لأنفسها. ومنهم لا يسلم خروج المبادئ عن المقولات؛ بل بجميع المبدأ أو ذا المبدأ في مقولة واحدة، ونقول إن الوحدة من جملة الكم والواحد في العدد، والعدد كم، وكذلك النقطة في الخط والخط كم. وأما الأعدام فملكاتها من المقولات كالعمى من الكيف والسكون من أن ينفعل، وطبائعه / من المختلفين يجعلون للشيء الواحد مقولات كثيرة، فيقولون مثلا إن النقطة من حيث هي طرف الخط من المضاف، ومن حيث هي هيئة ما من الكيف؛ وإن الشمال من حيث هو جسم من الجوهر، ومن حيث هو متحرك من أن
Chapter: On what is in the minds of things that are distinct from these ten. And what is in the minds of things that are distinct from these ten, such as unity which is the principle of number and the point which is a principle in a certain way for magnitudes, and also like matter and form, and also like privations such as blindness and ignorance and the like. Some of them have mentioned in this section particular examples, like north, south, morning meal, evening meal; so we say that not every existence of things does not enter into the categories as a symbol that the categories are ten, and it is not necessary at first sight that for every existing essence there is something that shares in essence existent so that that essence is existent, so it is not necessary that for every thing there is a species predicated on many in actuality. And if for every thing there is a species predicated on many by number, it is not necessary that with that species there be another species sharing in a common quiddity so that there is a genus. So it is not impossible that there be singular things with no partner in their species, and species that are species relative to what is below them but have no relation to what is above them. For if there are singular individuals with no species at all and no genera according to the mentioned condition, and species with no genera likewise, then none of that enters into a category from these categories. And what was said is correct, that the categories are these ten; because what is outside them is not a category in itself nor in a category of another, so if those things are outside the categories, that does not harm their being ten, unless it is established that those things have genera outside the ten. And moreover, among the well-known answers about them is what is transmitted from some of them that these things are outside the ten, especially what is in the manner of principles like unity, point, matter, and form; because principles do not enter into any category since they are the principles of them; and if they entered into them, they would be principles of themselves. And some of them do not accept the exclusion of principles from the categories; rather they say that the principle or the thing having the principle is in one category, and we say that unity is part of quantity, and one is in number, and number is quantity, and similarly the point is in the line and the line is quantity. As for privations, their dispositions are from the categories, like blindness is from quality and rest is from to be acted upon, and their natures... from the different ones make for one thing many categories, so they say for example that the point, in terms of being the end of the line, is from relation, and in terms of being a certain disposition, is from quality; and that north, in terms of being a body, is from substance, and in terms of being moving, is from to be acted upon.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage addresses entities that seem to fall outside the ten categories, such as unity (principle of number), point (principle of magnitude), matter and form (hyle and form), and privations like blindness. The author first explains that not every existing thing must have a species predicated on many in actuality—some things may be unique in their species or lack a higher genus. Thus they may not belong to any category without undermining the claim that categories are ten. The author then presents responses: some hold that principles like unity and point are outside categories because they are principles; if they were in a category, they would be principles of themselves. Others argue that principles do belong to categories (e.g., unity is quantity, point is quantity as part of a line). For privations, their corresponding dispositions (malakāt) fall under categories (e.g., blindness from quality, rest from passion). Finally, the author notes that some scholars assign multiple categories to a single thing, e.g., the point as relation and quality, or north as substance and passion.

Key Terms
الوحدة
النقطة
الهيولى
الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is one argument for why principles like unity are not in any category?
A) Because they are too simple to be classified.
B) Because if they were in a category, they would be principles of themselves.
C) Because the categories only apply to material things.
D) Because they are mental constructs only.

Key Takeaway
Principles are excluded from the categories because their inclusion would lead to a self-principle contradiction, but some scholars reintegrate them by placing them under quantity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
A mutakallim debates whether 'existence' is a category. Using the logic from this passage, argue whether existence is a principle (like unity) or falls under substance.

Privations and the Multi-Category Approach
Author analyzes privations and multi-category classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze how privations like blindness are excluded from the categories, and how some scholars assign a single thing to multiple categories based on different aspects.

Core Idea
Privations are not in categories; their corresponding dispositions are, but the lack itself is a non-being.

Privations—blindness, ignorance, rest—seem to be real absences, not positive entities. How can they fit into a scheme designed for positive existences? The author presents two moves: either privations are outside categories (their dispositions are in), or a single entity can belong to multiple categories under different descriptions. Let's unpack these logical strategies.

Guided Path
1
Identify privations mentioned
Find blindness, ignorance, rest in the Arabic. Note they are 'adam (non-being) of a disposition.
Privations are lacks, not positive features.
2
Understand the disposition argument
The text says privations have dispositions (malakat) that are in categories: blindness in quality, rest in passion.
The privation itself is not categorized; its opposite is.
3
Examine the multi-category example
The point can be in relation (as end of line) and in quality (as disposition). North can be substance (body) and passion (moving).
One thing can be in multiple categories under different descriptions.
4
Assess the logical coherence
Is it legitimate for one thing to have multiple categories? Does that violate category mutual exclusivity?
Consider whether categories are meant to be exclusive or just different perspectives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في ما يكون في الأوهام من الأمور المباينة لهذه العشرة] وما يكون في الأوهام من الأمور المباينة لهذه العشرة، فذلك كالوحدة التي هي مبدأ العدد والنقطة التي هي مبدأ بوجه ما للمقادير، وأيضًا مثل الهيولى والصورة، وأيضًا مثل الأعدام كالعمى والجهل وما أشبه ذلك. فمنهم من أورد في هذا الباب أمثلة جزئية، نحو الشمال والجنوب والغداء والعشاء؛ فنقول إنه ليس كل وجود الأشياء لا يدخل في المقولات رمز (۱) في أن المقولات عشرة، وليس يجب في بادي الرأي أن يكون لكل ذات موجودة ما يشاركه في الذات موجودًا حتى تكون تلك الذات موجودة، فليس يجب أن يكون لكل شيء نوع مقول على كثيرين بالفعل. ولو كان لكل شيء نوع مقول على كثيرين بالعدد لم يجب أن يكون مع ذلك النوع نوع آخر مشارك له في ماهية مشتركة حتى يكون هناك جنس. فلا يمتنع أن تكون أمور مفردة لا مشارك لها في نوعها، وأنواع هي أنواع بالقياس إلى ما تحتها ولا قياس لها إلى ما فوقها، فإن كانت أشخاص مفردة لا أنواع لها البتة ولا أجناس على الشرط المذكورة، وأنواع لا أجناس لها كذلك لم يكن شيء من ذلك داخلا في مقولة من هذه المقولات. وكان حقًا ما قيل إن المقولات هي هذه العشرة؛ إذ الخارج عنها ليس بمقولة في نفسه ولا في مقولة غيرها، فلو كانت تلك الأشياء خارجة عن المقولات فذلك لا يقدح في كونها عشرة، إلا وأن يصح أن لتلك الأشياء أجناسًا خارجة عن العشرة. وبعد فإن من الأجوبة المشهورة عنها ما نقل عن البعض منهم أن هذه الأشياء خارجة عن العشرة وخصوصًا ما كان منها يجري مجرى المبادئ كالوحدة والنقطة والهيولى والصورة؛ إذ المبادئ لا يدخل في شيء من المقولات لما أنها هي المبادئ لها؛ ولو دخلت فيها لكانت هي مبادئ لأنفسها. ومنهم لا يسلم خروج المبادئ عن المقولات؛ بل بجميع المبدأ أو ذا المبدأ في مقولة واحدة، ونقول إن الوحدة من جملة الكم والواحد في العدد، والعدد كم، وكذلك النقطة في الخط والخط كم. وأما الأعدام فملكاتها من المقولات كالعمى من الكيف والسكون من أن ينفعل، وطبائعه / من المختلفين يجعلون للشيء الواحد مقولات كثيرة، فيقولون مثلا إن النقطة من حيث هي طرف الخط من المضاف، ومن حيث هي هيئة ما من الكيف؛ وإن الشمال من حيث هو جسم من الجوهر، ومن حيث هو متحرك من أن

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider 'silence' (samt). Is it a privation of speech? What category would its disposition (speech) belong to? Can silence itself be in a category? Use the text's logic.

The Ten Categories

10 min
Critique of the Claim That Principles Are Outside the Categories
Author critiques the view that principles like unity and point are outside the categories, offering counterarguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical problem with the claim that principles (mabadi') are outside the categories and explain the author's refutation using the distinction between causal and non-causal principles.

Core Idea
Being a principle does not automatically exclude something from its own category.

In this passage, the author challenges a common objection that certain principles—unity, point, hyle, and form—must lie outside the ten categories because they are principles of those categories. He carefully dismantles this claim by distinguishing between principles that are causes and principles that are mere extremities, and by showing that even causal principles can belong to the same category as what they ground. This lesson will guide you through his critical argument and help you apply it to similar cases in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find the statement that 'principles do not enter into any of the categories because they are the principles for them.' Understand why someone might think this.
The claim is that if something is a principle of a category, it cannot itself be in that category, otherwise it would be a principle of itself.
2
Distinguish different types of principles
The author distinguishes between a principle as a cause (like unity for number) and a principle as an extremity (like a point for a line). Note that the point is not a cause of the line; it is merely an endpoint.
This distinction is crucial: only causal principles might have a stronger claim to being outside, but even they are not automatically excluded.
3
Apply the distinction to unity and point
Unity is a principle for discrete quantity (number) both as cause and as extremity; the point is a principle only as an extremity for continuous quantity. Therefore, the point cannot be a principle for the whole category of quantity, only for a part.
The author shows that the original objection fails because the principles are not principles for the entire category.
4
Examine the case of hyle and form
Hyle and form are principles of natural bodies but are themselves in the category of substance. Show that being a principle does not prevent sharing the same category with what it principles.
The author argues that if the principle argument were valid, then the line could not share a genus with surface and body, which is absurd.

المنطقة الكبيرة
موجودًا حتى تكون تلك الذات موجودة، فليس يجب أن يكون لكل شيء نوع مقول على كثيرين بالفعل. ولو كان لكل شيء نوع مقول على كثيرين بالعدد لم يجب أن يكون مع ذلك النوع نوع آخر مشارك له في ماهية مشتركة حتى يكون هناك جنس. فلا يمتنع أن تكون أمور مفردة لا مشارك لها في نوعها، وأنواع هي أنواع بالقياس إلى ما تحتها ولا قياس لها إلى ما فوقها، فإن كانت أشخاص مفردة لا أنواع لها البتة ولا أجناس على الشرط المذكورة، وأنواع لا أجناس لها كذلك لم يكن شيء من ذلك داخلا في مقولة من هذه المقولات. وكان حقًا ما قيل إن المقولات هي هذه العشرة؛ إذ الخارج عنها ليس بمقولة في نفسه ولا في مقولة غيرها، فلو كانت تلك الأشياء خارجة عن المقولات فذلك لا يقدح في كونها عشرة، إلا وأن يصح أن لتلك الأشياء أجناسًا خارجة عن العشرة.
وبعد فإن من الأجوبة المشهورة عنها ما نقل عن البعض منهم أن هذه الأشياء خارجة عن العشرة وخصوصًا ما كان منها يجري مجرى المبادئ كالوحدة والنقطة والهيولى والصورة؛ إذ المبادئ لا يدخل في شيء من المقولات لما أنها هي المبادئ لها؛ ولو دخلت فيها لكانت هي مبادئ لأنفسها. ومنهم لا يسلم خروج المبادئ عن المقولات؛ بل بجميع المبدأ أو ذا المبدأ في مقولة واحدة، ونقول إن الوحدة من جملة الكم والواحد في العدد، والعدد كم، وكذلك النقطة في الخط والخط كم. وأما الأعدام فملكاتها [40] من المقولات كالعمى من الكيف والسكون من أن ينفعل، وطبائعه من المختلفين يجعلون للشيء الواحد مقولات كثيرة، فيقولون مثلا إن النقطة من حيث هي طرف الخط من المضاف، ومن حيث هي هيئة ما من الكيف؛ وإن الشمال من حيث هو جسم من الجوهر، ومن حيث هو متحرك من أن ينفعل، ومن حيث هو مختص بأحد النقطتين من الأين، والتعدي من حيث هو تحريك من أن يفعل، ومن حيث هو للمتعدي من المضاف، ومن حيث هو في زمان مخصوص من متى.
فعلينا أن نتأمل فيما ذهبوا إليه فنقول: أما قولهم في الأول «إن هذه المبادئ مبادئ للمقولة بأسرها فلا يكون من المقولة، فذلك باطل؛ إذ الوحدة لا يكون مبدءًا للكمية بأسرها بل للمنفصل، والنقطة أيضًا لا تكون مبدءًا للكمية بأسرها بل للمتصل، وليس حال النقطة كالوحدة. فإن الوحدة مبدأ للعدد على أنها علة ومبدأ على أنها طرف، وليست النقطة كذلك، فإنها ليست علة للمقدار البتة؛ بل هي مبدأ على أنها طرف، ومن زعم أنها علة للخط فذلك بمعزل عن الحقائق. ومع ذلك فإنهما إذا كانتا مبدأين لم تكن نفس كونهما مبدأين يوجب أن لا يكونا من الكم حتى يكون الكم أعم من المتصل والمنفصل، وإنهما من المبادئ المتصفة بصفة العلية للمتصل والمنفصل. فلو كانت الكمية منحصرة في المتصل والمنفصل فقد كانتا أي النقطة والوحدة مبدأين للكمية بأسرها وإلا فلا، وهذا على الحقيقة يعرف عند تعريف الكمية وتحقيقها من بعد. وأما الجوهر فقد مرَّ من قبل أنه هو الموجود لا في موضوع وهذا الرسم يكون مقولاً على الهيولى والصورة قولاً ذاتياً فتكونا داخلتين إذن في مقولة الجوهر، وهما مبدئان لبعض ما يقال عليه المقولة وهو الأجسام الطبيعية، فليس كون الشيء مبدءًا مانعا (۱) عن أن يشارك ما هو له مبدءًا في المقولة. ولو كانت النقطة يمتنع أن تشارك المقادير في الجنس إلا على الذي هو الكم لكونها مبدأ لكان الخط أيضًا يمتنع أن يشارك السطح والجسم في الجنس الأقرب؛
(۱) النسخة: مانع. وما أثبتناه هو الصواب كما هو الظاهر من السياق.
The Great Logic. ... exists so that that essence exists, so it is not necessary that for every thing there is a species predicated of many in actuality. And if for every thing there is a species predicated of many in number, it would not be necessary that with that species there be another species sharing a common quiddity so that there be a genus. So it is not impossible that there be singular things with no partner in their species, and species that are species relative to what is under them and have no relation to what is above them. For if there were singular individuals with no species at all and no genera on the mentioned condition, and species with no genera likewise, then nothing of that would enter into a category from among these categories. And what was said is true: that the categories are these ten; since what is outside them is not a category in itself nor in another category. So if those things were outside the categories, that does not detract from their being ten, unless it be valid that those things have genera outside the ten. After that, among the well-known replies about them is what is transmitted from some of them that these things are outside the ten, especially what runs in the manner of principles like unity, point, hyle, and form; since principles do not enter into any of the categories because they are the principles for them; and if they entered into them, they would be principles for themselves. And among them are those who do not concede the exit of principles from the categories; rather, with all of the principle or the possessor of the principle in one category, and we say that unity is from the category of quantity and the one in number, and number is quantity, and similarly the point in the line and the line is quantity. As for privations, their possessings [40] are from the categories, like blindness from quality and rest from being affected, and their natures from those who differ make for one thing many categories, so they say, for example, that the point, insofar as it is the extremity of the line, belongs to relation; insofar as it is a certain quality, belongs to quality; and that the north, insofar as it is a body, belongs to substance; insofar as it is moving, belongs to being affected; insofar as it is specific to one of the two points, belongs to where; and the transitive, insofar as it is a movement, belongs to doing; insofar as it is for the transitive, belongs to relation; and insofar as it is in a specific time, belongs to when. So we must reflect on what they have gone to and we say: As for their statement in the first [argument] that 'these principles are principles for the entire category so they are not from the category,' that is false; because unity is not a principle for the whole quantity but for the discrete, and the point also is not a principle for the whole quantity but for the continuous, and the state of the point is not like unity. For unity is a principle for number as a cause and as an extremity, but the point is not like that; it is not a cause for magnitude at all; rather it is a principle as an extremity, and whoever claims it is a cause for the line is far from the realities. Moreover, when they are principles, their being principles does not necessitate that they are not from quantity such that quantity is more general than continuous and discrete, and they are from the principles described with the attribute of causality for the continuous and discrete. For if quantity were confined to continuous and discrete, then they, i.e. the point and unity, would be principles for the whole quantity, otherwise not. And this is truly known when defining quantity and verifying it later. As for substance, it has passed before that it is the existent not in a subject; and this description is said of hyle and form essentially; so they then enter into the category of substance, and they are principles for some of what the category is said of, i.e., natural bodies. So the fact that a thing is a principle does not prevent it from sharing the category with that for which it is a principle. And if it were impossible for the point to share with magnitudes in genus except that which is quantity because it is a principle, then the line also would be impossible to share with surface and body in the nearest genus. (1) The copy: [مانع] and what we have established is correct as is apparent from the context.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the view that certain principles—namely unity, point, hyle (matter), and form—fall outside the ten Aristotelian categories because they are principles. The author first restates an objection: if these are principles of the categories, they cannot themselves be within the categories, as that would make them principles of themselves. He then presents two responses: some thinkers accept that these principles are outside the ten, while others try to include them by placing unity in quantity, point in quantity, etc., and also discuss privations and the practice of assigning multiple categories to a single thing. The author then gives his own detailed refutation. He argues that the claim that 'principles are principles for the entire category' is false because unity is only a principle for discrete quantity (number) and not for continuous quantity, and the point is only a principle for continuous quantity as an extremity, not as a cause. He distinguishes between a principle as a cause and a principle as an extremity, showing that the point is not a cause of magnitude. He further argues that even if they were principles, being a principle does not preclude belonging to the same category; for example, hyle and form are principles for natural bodies yet they fall under substance. He concludes that if the principle argument were valid, it would lead to absurdities like the line not sharing a genus with surface and body. The passage is structured as a naqd (objection) and jawab (response), with the author systematically dismantling the initial claim.

Key Terms
مقولة (maqūla): One of the ten highest genera or categories that classify all existents (e.g., substance, quantity, quality). [Note: Stable term across the logical tradition, though later logicians may differ on the number or definitions.]
مبدأ (mabda'): A principle; that which is a source or foundation for something else, either as a cause or as an extremity (like a point for a line). [Note: In this context, mabda' distinguishes between causal principles (e.g., unity as cause of number) and non-causal principles (e.g., point as extremity).]
وحدة (waḥda): Unity; considered a principle of number (discrete quantity), and itself falls under the category of quantity. [Note: No significant shift; unity is standardly in the category of quantity in Aristotelian logic.]
نقطة (nuqṭa): Point; a principle of the line (continuous quantity) as an extremity, not as a cause; argued here to be within the category of quantity. [Note: No significant shift; point is typically in the category of quantity as a limit.]
هيولى (hayūlā): Hyle or prime matter; a principle of natural bodies along with form; argued here to be within the category of substance. [Note: In later Islamic philosophy, hayūlā is often discussed separately but remains a principle of substance.]
صورة (ṣūra): Form; the other principle of natural bodies; also within the category of substance according to the author. [Note: Same as above.]
كم (kamm): Quantity; one of the ten categories, divided into discrete (number) and continuous (line, surface, etc.). [Note: Standard across the tradition.]
جوهر (jawhar): Substance; the category of existents that are not in a subject, including hyle and form. [Note: Standard; though some theologians redefine substance differently.]

Refutation of Unity and Point as Principles of All Quantity
Author critiques the claim that unity and point are principles for all quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical mistake in claiming that unity and point are principles for all quantity and explain why they are not excluded from the category.

Core Idea
The claim that unity and point are principles for all quantity fails because they each apply only to one subdivision of quantity.

In this passage, the author examines a claim made by some logicians: that unity and point are principles for the entire category of quantity, and therefore cannot themselves be quantities. This is an important critical move in the science of logic, because it shows how careful analysis of definitions and scope can reveal hidden assumptions. We will walk through the author's refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being attacked
The logicians claim that unity (wahda) and point (nuqta) are principles (mabadi') for the whole category of quantity, and therefore they are not themselves quantities. Locate where this claim is stated in the Arabic.
The claim is introduced with 'أما قولهم في الأول «إن هذه المبادئ مبادئ للمقولة بأسرها فلا يكون من المقولة»'
2
Break down the refutation of unity
The author argues that unity is only a principle for discrete quantity (number), not for continuous quantity. It cannot be a principle for all quantity. Check the Arabic: why does the author say unity is not a principle for all?
The author says: 'إذ الوحدة لا يكون مبدءًا للكمية بأسرها بل للمنفصل'
3
Analyse the distinction between unity and point
The author points out an asymmetry: unity is a principle for number both as a cause and as an extremity, but the point is only an extremity, not a cause. Understand why this matters for the refutation.
The author says: 'فإن الوحدة مبدأ للعدد على أنها علة ومبدأ على أنها طرف، وليست النقطة كذلك'
4
Assess the conclusion about being in the category
The author concludes that being a principle does not automatically exclude something from the category. Unity itself is part of number. Evaluate: if quantity is defined as including both continuous and discrete, then these are principles for all quantity, otherwise not.
The final sentence: 'فلو كانت الكمية منحصرة في المتصل والمنفصل فقد كانتا أي النقطة والوحدة مبدأين للكمية بأسرها وإلا فلا'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلينا أن نتأمل فيما ذهبوا إليه فنقول: أما قولهم في الأول «إن هذه المبادئ مبادئ للمقولة بأسرها فلا يكون من المقولة، فذلك باطل؛ إذ الوحدة لا يكون مبدءًا للكمية بأسرها بل للمنفصل، والنقطة أيضًا لا تكون مبدءًا للكمية بأسرها بل للمتصل، وليس حال النقطة كالوحدة. فإن الوحدة مبدأ للعدد على أنها علة ومبدأ على أنها طرف، وليست النقطة كذلك، فإنها ليست علة للمقدار البتة؛ بل هي مبدأ على أنها طرف، ومن زعم أنها علة للخط فذلك بمعزل عن الحقائق. ومع ذلك فإنهما إذا كانتا مبدأين لم تكن نفس كونهما مبدأين يوجب أن لا يكونا من الكم حتى يكون الكم أعم من المتصل والمنفصل، وإنهما من المبادئ المتصفة بصفة العلية للمتصل والمنفصل. فلو كانت الكمية منحصرة في المتصل والمنفصل فقد كانتا أي النقطة والوحدة مبدأين للكمية بأسرها وإلا فلا، وهذا على الحقيقة يعرف عند تعريف الكمية وتحقيقها من بعد.
We must examine what they have maintained and say: As for their first statement that 'these principles are principles for the whole category so they are not from the category,' that is false; because unity is not a principle for all of quantity but for the discrete, and the point also is not a principle for all of quantity but for the continuous, and the case of the point is not like that of unity. For unity is a principle for number as a cause and as an extremity, but the point is not like that; it is not a cause for magnitude at all; rather it is a principle as an extremity, and whoever claims it is a cause for the line is far from the truths. Moreover, if they are principles, their very being principles does not require that they are not from quantity so that quantity is more general than the continuous and the discrete; rather they are among the principles characterized by causality for the continuous and the discrete. So if quantity were confined to the continuous and the discrete, then the point and unity would be principles for all of quantity, otherwise not, and this is truly known when quantity is defined and verified later.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation. The author examines the claim that unity (wahda) and point (nuqta) are principles for the entire category of quantity (kammiyya) and therefore cannot themselves be quantities. The author argues that this claim is false. First, unity is a principle only for discrete quantity (number), not for continuous quantity. Second, the point is a principle only for continuous quantity (line), not for discrete quantity. Therefore, they are not principles for all quantity. Furthermore, the author distinguishes between unity and point: unity is a principle for number both as a cause and as an extremity (i.e., unit), whereas the point is only an extremity, not a cause. The key logical point is that being a principle does not automatically exclude something from being in the category. For example, unity itself is part of number. The author concludes that whether these are principles for all quantity depends on whether quantity is defined as including both continuous and discrete.

Substance as Genus for Hyle and Form
Author argues that substance is a genus for hyle and form despite their being principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rasm of substance (existence not in a subject) to hyle and form and justify their inclusion in the category of substance despite their being principles.

Core Idea
A principle can share a genus with its product — the rasm of substance covers both hyle and form.

In this passage, the author defends the seemingly paradoxical claim that hyle (matter) and form (sura) are substances, even though they are the internal principles of natural bodies. He uses the description (rasm) of substance as 'existence not in a subject' to show that they fit, and then argues by analogy that being a principle does not remove something from its genus.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of substance
The author reminds us that substance (jawhar) is defined as 'existence not in a subject' (al-mawjud la fi mawdu'). This is a rasm (description) using a negative accidental property, not an essential definition.
This rasm is coextensive with substance for the Aristotelian categories.
2
Identify the claim about hyle and form
The author asserts that this rasm is predicated essentially (qawlan dhatiyyan) on hyle and form. Therefore, hyle and form are included in the category of substance.
3
Recognize the potential objection
The objection: hyle and form are principles (mabadi') of natural bodies — how can something be both a principle and in the same category as its product?
4
Examine the author's rebuttal
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum. He assumes the rule 'if something is a principle, it cannot share the genus with its product' and shows it leads to contradictions: a point would be excluded from quantity, but then a line would be excluded from magnitude, which is false. Likewise, ten would be excluded from number.
This shows the rule is false; principles can share genera.
5
Conclude the logical point
The rasm of substance is broad enough to include principles. The key is that the definition applies essentially to them, regardless of their role as principles. Thus, hyle and form are substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجوهر فقد مرَّ من قبل أنه هو الموجود لا في موضوع وهذا الرسم يكون مقولاً على الهيولى والصورة قولاً ذاتياً فتكونا داخلتين إذن في مقولة الجوهر، وهما مبدئان لبعض ما يقال عليه المقولة وهو الأجسام الطبيعية، فليس كون الشيء مبدءًا مانعًا عن أن يشارك ما هو له مبدءًا في المقولة. ولو كانت النقطة يمتنع أن تشارك المقادير في الجنس إلا على الذي هو الكم لكونها مبدأ لكان الخط أيضًا يمتنع أن يشارك السطح والجسم في الجنس الأقرب؛ وهو المقدار. وكذلك كانت العشرة يمتنع أن تشارك المائة في الجنس الأقرب؛ وهو العدد فإن العشرة من المائة كالواحد من العشرة.
As for substance, it has already been mentioned that it is existence not in a subject, and this description (rasm) is predicated of hyle and form essentially, so both of them are included in the category of substance, although they are principles for some of that upon which the category is predicated, namely natural bodies. So a thing being a principle does not prevent it from sharing the category with that for which it is a principle. And if the point were prevented from sharing with magnitudes in the genus except on account of its being a principle of quantity, then the line would also be prevented from sharing with surface and body in the nearest genus, which is magnitude. Likewise, ten would be prevented from sharing with hundred in the nearest genus, which is number, for ten is to hundred as the unit is to ten.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage defines the relation of substance (jawhar) to its principles, hyle (hayula) and form (sura). The author reminds us that substance is defined as 'existence not in a subject' (al-mawjud la fi mawdu'), and this description applies essentially to both hyle and form. Therefore, they fall under the category of substance, even though they are principles for natural bodies. The key point is that being a principle does not prevent something from sharing the same genus as its product. The author illustrates with counterexamples: if being a principle excluded a thing from the genus, then the point would be excluded from quantity, but then the line (which is also a principle for surface and body) would also be excluded from magnitude, which is absurd. Similarly, ten (which is a principle for hundred in the sense of being a component) would be excluded from number if the same logic applied, yet ten is a number. Thus, the argument shows that principles can belong to the same category as their derivative.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar): Substance; defined as existence not in a subject; a category that includes hyle, form, and body.
هيولى (hayula): Hyle, matter; a principle of natural bodies, but itself considered substance.
صورة (sura): Form; a principle of natural bodies, also considered substance.
مبدأ (mabda'): Principle; here, an internal constituent (hyle/form) that is not necessarily excluded from the category of its product.
رسم (rasm): Description; a non-essential definition using accidents, here applied to substance.

Key Takeaway
The rasm of substance as 'existence not in a subject' encompasses internal principles like hyle and form, and being a principle does not exclude something from the genus of its product.

Priority and Posteriority in Genus Predication
Author addresses objection about priority/posteriority preventing genus predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between priority in concept and priority in existence and determine whether each prevents a term from being a genus.

Core Idea
Priority in a concept prevents genus predication; priority in existence does not.

You have learned that a genus must be predicated equally on its species. But what if one species is prior to another? Does that break the genus? In this passage, the author addresses an objection based on the priority of hyle and form over body, and clarifies a crucial distinction that every logician must master.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the objector's claim that since 'existent' is not a genus due to priority/posteriority, substance cannot be a genus for hyle and form because they are prior to body.
The objector uses the earlier ruling on 'existent' as a precedent.
2
Extract the author's distinction
The author distinguishes two types of priority/posteriority: one in the concept itself (e.g., substance over accident) and one in something external, such as existence (e.g., father over son).
The key question: is the priority part of the meaning of the genus?
3
Apply the distinction to the examples
For father and son, 'humanity' applies equally as a definition; the priority is in existence, not in the concept. Similarly, hyle and form are prior to body in existence, but the concept of substance applies equally to all three.
The author emphasizes that body is substance by itself, not because of its parts.
4
Evaluate the objection
The objection fails because the priority of hyle and form is not in the concept of substance. Therefore, substance remains a valid genus for hyle, form, and body.
This shows that only priority in the meaning of a term prevents it from being a genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول قد بينتم من قبل أن الموجود ليس بجنس لما أن وقوعه على ما تحته من المقولات يتقدم ويتأخر. وإنه مما ينافي كون الجوهر جنسا للهيولى والصورة والجسم؛ وقد كان كل واحد من الهيولى والصورة أقدم بالطبع من الجسم فليس قول الجوهر عليها بالسوية بل يتقدم ويتأخر. وقد يعرض هذا في الغير أيضًا. فإن البعض من الكميات قبل بعض كالخط، فإنه قبل السطح والثلاثة قبل الأربعة، وعسى أن يكون الأمر كذلك في أنواع أخرى من المقولات. فنقول إن التقدم والتأخر في جزئيات يشتملها معنى واحد لا يخلو من أن يكونا في المفهوم لها من ذلك المعنى أو في الغير كمفهوم النوع أو الجنس : أما الأول فذلك مثل تقدم الجوهر على العرض، وأما الثاني فمثل تقدم الإنسان الذي هو الأب على الذي هو الابن، فإن حد الإنسان من حيث هو حد لهما بالسوية، وإن كان وجود الإنسانية للأب قبل بالزمان إلا في أنها إنسانية بل في أنها موجودة. وبالجملة فما يجعل الابن إنسانًا فذلك لماهية إنسان فإن من المحال أن لا يكون الابن إنسانا؛ فلذلك لا علة له في أنه إنسان بخلاف كونه موجودًا فإنه لا بعلة لا محالة، ومن حق الجنس أن يقال على أنواعه بالسوية. وأما الثالث فذلك ليس يمتنع، ولا مانع من التشابه في الشركة ولذلك لا يباين الأبُ الابن في مقولة الجوهر. وكذلك الحال في نسبة الهيولى والصورة إلى الجسم؛ فإن الجسم لذاته جوهر لا لسبب ما لكنه في وجوده محتاج إلى أسباب، فجوهرية الشيء في أنها جوهرية لا تكون علة لجوهرية شيء آخر حتى يكون الجسم لجوهرية المادة والصورة جوهرا، لستُ أقول
Then someone may say: You have previously shown that 'existent' is not a genus because its predication on the categories under it is with priority and posteriority. And this contradicts substance being a genus for hyle, form, and body; for each of hyle and form is naturally prior to body, so the predication of substance upon them is not equal but with priority and posteriority. And this occurs in other cases too. For some quantities are prior to others, like the line is prior to the surface, and three is prior to four, and perhaps it is the same in other categories. We say: Priority and posteriority in particulars that are included under one meaning are either in the concept of that meaning or in something else, like the concept of species or genus. As for the first, it is like the priority of substance over accident. As for the second, it is like the priority of the father over the son in humanity; for the definition of man, as a definition, applies equally to both, although the existence of humanity in the father is prior in time, but that priority is not in humanity but in existence. In summary, what makes the son a man is the essence of man, for it is impossible for the son not to be a man; therefore there is no cause for his being a man, unlike his existence, which necessarily has a cause. And it is the right of a genus to be predicated equally on its species. As for the third [case of priority in existence], that is not impossible, and there is no impediment to sharing the genus; therefore the father does not differ from the son in the category of substance. Similarly, the case holds for the relation of hyle and form to body: for body is substance by itself, not for a cause, though in its existence it needs causes; so the substantially of a thing, insofar as it is substantially, does not cause the substantially of another thing such that body is substance due to the substantially of matter and form.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical response to an objection. The objector argues that since 'existent' (mawjud) is not a genus due to priority/posteriority (taqaddum/ta'akhkhur), substance cannot be a genus for hyle, form, and body because hyle and form are naturally prior. The author clarifies that priority/posteriority can be of two types: (1) in the concept itself (like substance prior to accident), which prevents generic predication; (2) in something external, like the temporal priority of father over son in humanity. In the latter, the genus definition applies equally to both; the priority is in existence, not in the essence. Therefore, substance can still be a genus for hyle and form despite their priority. The author further explains that body is substance by itself, not due to the substantially of its parts. Thus, the objection fails because the priority is not in the genus concept but in temporal existence, which does not preclude equal predication of the genus.

Key Terms
تقدم (taqaddum): Priority; here, two types: in concept (e.g., substance over accident) and in existence (e.g., father over son).
تأخر (ta'akhkhur): Posteriority; the opposite of taqaddum.
جنس (jins): Genus; is predicated equally on its species; requires that the concept apply without priority/posteriority.
موجود (mawjud): Existent; not a genus because of priority/posteriority in its concept.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the temporal priority of hyle and form over body not prevent substance from being their genus?
A) Because priority and posteriority always prevent generic predication.
B) Because the priority is in existence, not in the genus concept.
C) Because hyle and form are not really prior.
D) Because body is not a substance.

Key Takeaway
Priority in the concept of a genus prevents equal predication; priority in existence does not, so genus predication remains valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following: 'Humanity' is predicated on Adam and on his descendants, though Adam existed first. Is 'humanity' still a genus? Apply the distinction from this lesson and explain.
?
In Islamic jurisprudence, the category 'worship act' ('ibada) is predicated on prayer and fasting, though prayer was prescribed before fasting. Does temporal priority prevent them from sharing the genus? Use the author's reasoning.

Priority in Concept vs. Existence: Defending Genus
Author refutes an objection that priority/posteriority invalidates genus by distinguishing existence from concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between priority in concept and priority in existence when evaluating whether a universal qualifies as a genus.

Core Idea
Only priority in concept invalidates a genus; priority in existence does not.

In this passage, the author faces an objection: if a universal applies with priority and posteriority, it cannot be a genus. For example, substance seems to apply to matter and form with priority. The author's reply introduces a critical distinction: priority may be in the concept (like substance over accident) or in existence (like father over son). Only the former weakens the claim to genus. This distinction is essential for understanding how universals like 'substance' and 'number' remain valid genera despite apparent inequalities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
What is the objection raised against substance and number being genera? The objector argues that priority and posteriority among their species (e.g., matter and form under substance, three and four under number) indicate they cannot be true genera.
The objection relies on the principle that a genus must be predicated equally.
2
Locate the author's examples
Find the examples the author uses to illustrate priority in existence: father and son (both human), matter and form (both substance), and three and four (both number). In each, one is prior in existence but not in concept.
These examples show that the common definition remains the same.
3
Trace the distinction
How does the author separate priority in concept from priority in existence? Priority in concept occurs when the common meaning itself is not equally applicable (e.g., substance vs. accident). Priority in existence occurs when the essence is the same but the existence of one depends on the other (e.g., father exists before son).
Only the former violates the equality required for a genus.
4
Apply the distinction
Why does priority in existence not break the equality required for a genus? Because the definition of the universal (e.g., 'human') applies to both father and son equally; the temporal priority does not affect the essence. Therefore, the genus remains valid.
This principle is crucial for defending genera in categories like substance and quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو المقدار. وكذلك كانت العشرة يمتنع أن تشارك المائة في الجنس الأقرب؛ وهو العدد فإن العشرة من المائة كالواحد من العشرة.
ثم لقائل أن يقول قد بينتم من قبل أن الموجود ليس بجنس لما أن وقوعه على ما تحته من المقولات يتقدم ويتأخر. وإنه مما ينافي كون الجوهر جنسا للهيولى والصورة والجسم؛ وقد كان كل واحد من الهيولى والصورة أقدم بالطبع من الجسم فليس قول الجوهر عليها بالسوية بل يتقدم ويتأخر. وقد يعرض هذا في الغير أيضًا. فإن البعض من الكميات قبل بعض كالخط، فإنه قبل السطح والثلاثة قبل الأربعة، وعسى أن يكون الأمر كذلك في أنواع أخرى من المقولات. فنقول إن التقدم والتأخر في جزئيات يشتملها معنى واحد لا يخلو من أن يكونا في المفهوم لها من ذلك المعنى أو في الغير كمفهوم النوع أو الجنس : أما الأول فذلك مثل تقدم الجوهر على العرض، وأما الثاني فمثل تقدم الإنسان الذي هو الأب على الذي هو الابن، فإن حد الإنسان من حيث هو حد لهما بالسوية، وإن كان وجود الإنسانية للأب قبل بالزمان إلا في أنها إنسانية بل في أنها موجودة. وبالجملة فما يجعل الابن إنسانًا فذلك لماهية إنسان فإن من المحال أن لا يكون الابن إنسانا؛ فلذلك لا علة له في أنه إنسان بخلاف كونه موجودًا فإنه لا بعلة لا محالة، ومن حق الجنس أن يقال على أنواعه بالسوية. وأما الثالث فذلك ليس يمتنع، ولا مانع من التشابه في الشركة ولذلك لا يباين الأبُ الابن في مقولة الجوهر. وكذلك الحال في نسبة الهيولى والصورة إلى الجسم؛ فإن الجسم لذاته جوهر لا لسبب ما لكنه في وجوده محتاج إلى أسباب، فجوهرية الشيء في أنها جوهرية لا تكون علة لجوهرية شيء آخر حتى يكون الجسم لجوهرية المادة والصورة جوهرا، لستُ أقول جوهرًا موجودًا . وكذلك الثنائية والثلاثية في كونهما عددًا؛ بل كل واحد من المثالين علة لما بعده في الوجود، فقد يكون وجود الشيء علة لوجود شيء آخر، ولذلك لا يكون الموجود جنسا؛ إذ معناه بوجوده الجوهر ثم بتوسطه للعرض. فيتبين إذن أن تُقدم الثلاثة على الأربعة، إنما هو في الوجود وهو غير معنى العدد، وكذلك تُقدم الهيولى والصورة على الجسم في الوجود وهو غير معنى الجوهرية. فبمعنى المقولة إذن إنما يتقدم الأنواع ويتأخر عنها لا لنفسه؛ بل لمعنى يضاف إليه فيه التقديم والتأخير وهو الوجود، فهذا أصل نافع في معرفة الفرق بين تقدم أنواع المقولة بعضها على بعض؛ وبين تقدم أصناف الموجود وما يجري مجراه بعضها على بعض.
It is the quantity. Similarly, the ten is prevented from sharing the nearest genus with the hundred, which is number, for the ten is to the hundred as the one is to the ten. Then a speaker may say: You previously showed that existence is not a genus because its applicability to what is under it from the categories is prior and posterior. And that is contradictory to substance being a genus for matter, form, and body; each of matter and form is prior by nature to body, so substance is not predicated of them equally but with priority and posteriority. And this also occurs in other things. For some quantities are prior to others, like the line is prior to the surface, and three is prior to four, and perhaps it is the case in other types of categories. So we say: Priority and posteriority in particulars that a single meaning encompasses, either they are in the concept of that meaning or in something else, like the concept of species or genus. As for the first, that is like the priority of substance over accident. As for the second, that is like the priority of the father human over the son human; the definition of human insofar as it is a definition applies to both equally, even if the existence of humanity for the father is prior in time, except that it is in humanity, rather in existence. In sum, what makes the son a human is the essence of human, for it is impossible that the son is not a human; therefore there is no cause for him in being human, unlike his being existent, which inevitably has a cause. And it is the right of the genus to be predicated of its species equally. As for the third, that is not impossible, nor is there an obstacle to similarity in participation, and therefore the father does not differ from the son in the category of substance. Similarly is the case of the relation of matter and form to body; for body is substance in itself not for a cause, but in its existence it is in need of causes. So the substantiality of a thing in that it is substantiality is not a cause for the substantiality of another thing such that body becomes substance for the substantiality of matter and form. I do not say existent substance. Likewise, duality and triplicity in that they are number; rather, each of the two examples is a cause for what is after it in existence. So existence of a thing may be a cause for the existence of another thing, and therefore existence is not a genus; for its meaning is that substance exists then through its intermediacy for accident. So it is evident that the priority of three over four is only in existence, which is not the meaning of number, and similarly the priority of matter and form over body is in existence, which is not the meaning of substantiality. So by the meaning of the category, the species are prior and posterior not due to itself, but due to a meaning added to it in which priority and posteriority lie, which is existence. This is a useful principle for knowing the difference between the priority of the types of a category over each other and the priority of the kinds of existence and what runs its course over each other.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: an objection and a detailed reply. The objection claims that priority and posteriority among individuals under a common meaning invalidate the equality required for a genus. The author systematically refutes this by distinguishing priority in concept from priority in existence. He first gives examples from quantity (line/surface, three/four) and from substance (father/son, matter-form/body). The key move: if priority is only in existence (like father over son), the common definition still applies equally; only priority in concept (like substance over accident) would disrupt genus. The author concludes that the priority of three over four is in existence, not in the meaning of number, and similarly for matter/form over body. Therefore, the genus remains valid. This principle helps differentiate between priority within a category and priority among existents.

Key Terms
جنس
تقدم
مقولة
موجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following is NOT a valid reason to reject a universal as a genus?
A) The universal applies with priority in concept.
B) The universal applies with priority in existence.
C) The universal does not apply equally to all its species.
D) The universal is not predicated in answer to 'what is it?'.

Key Takeaway
Priority in existence does not violate the equality required for a genus; only priority in concept does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'The number 3 is prior to the number 4 in the genus of number; therefore, number is not a true genus.' Using the author's distinction, explain whether this objection is valid and why.
?

Refuting the Claim That the Unit Belongs to Quantity
Author criticizes the argument that the unit is a quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the argument that something belongs to a category simply because it is found in that category, using logical and theological examples.

Core Idea
Being found in a category does not mean belonging to it; otherwise all accidents would be substances.

The author tackles a common logical fallacy: assuming that if a thing is found within a category, it must itself be part of that category. Using the example of the unit (wahda) and number, he shows why this reasoning fails. He then extends the principle to non-existence, clarifying that only essences belong to categories essentially.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The author refutes the claim that 'the unit belongs to quantity because number is quantity and the unit is in number.' Understand what the unit is and why this claim seems plausible.
The unit is the principle of number, not a quantity itself.
2
Extract the reductio ad absurdum
The author argues: if everything found in a category were from it, then all accidents (which are found in substances) would be substances. This is absurd, so the premise is false.
This is a classic logical move: show that the premise leads to an impossible conclusion.
3
Apply the distinction
The unit exists in number as a species exists in a genus, not as a part of the genus. Number is a quantity, but the unit is not a quantity itself.
The unit is a constituent of number, but does not share the defining property of quantity (extension).
4
Extend to non-existence
Non-existences that are opposites (like blindness) share categories, but true non-existences have no essential existence; they exist only accidentally in a subject. Thus, they do not belong to categories essentially.
Only essences (dhawat) and existential things are in categories per se.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم إن الوحدة في العدد، والعدد من الكم؛ فالوحدة من الكم، فذلك باطل؛ إذ ليس كل شيء يوجد في مقولة فهو منها وإلا فالأعراض كلها جواهر بل إذا كانت الوحدة موجودة في العدد وجود النوع في الجنس. ثم فإن العدد نوع من الكم يجب أن تكون الوحدة من مقولة الكم، وأما حال العدم فالعدم يقال على الضد وعلى غيره من الأعدام أيضًا؛ فالأعدام التي هي عبارة عن الأضداد أنها تشارك المقولات. وأما الأعدام الحقيقية فليست ذواتا بل أعدام ذوات والمقولات هي مقولات ذوات وأمور وجودية، والأعدام لا خصة لها من الوجود وإنما وجودها في موضوعها وجود بالعرض، فلو دخلت في مقولة دخلت بالعرض، وذلك لا يكون دخول النوع في المقولة؛ إذ النوع يدخل في جنسه بالذات.
As for their saying that the unit is in number, and number is from quantity, so the unit is from quantity—that is false. For not everything that exists in a category is from it; otherwise all accidents would be substances. Rather, if the unit exists in number as the existence of a species in a genus, then number is a type of quantity; the unit must be from the category of quantity. As for the case of non-existence: non-existence is predicated of the opposite and of other non-existences as well. The non-existences that are expressions of opposites share in the categories. As for true non-existences, they are not essences but rather non-existences of essences, and categories are categories of essences and existential matters, and non-existences have no share of existence; their existence in their subject is accidental existence. So if they entered a category, they would enter accidentally, and that is not the entry of a species into a category, for a species enters into its genus essentially.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the claim that the unit (al-wahda) belongs to the category of quantity because number is quantity. The author argues that not everything found in a category is itself in that category—otherwise accidents would be substances. The unit in number exists as a species exists in a genus, not as a member of that genus. The author then discusses non-existence: privations (like blindness) are opposites and share in categories, but true non-existences have no essential existence; they exist only accidentally in a subject, so they do not enter a category essentially. This underscores that only essences (dhawat) and existential things belong to categories essentially.

Key Terms
وحدة
عدم
بالعرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it false to say that the unit belongs to the category of quantity?
A) Because the unit is not a quantity, but a substance.
B) Because not everything found in a category is from that category; otherwise all accidents would be substances.
C) Because the unit is a non-existence.
D) Because number is not a quantity.

Key Takeaway
Not every entity found within a category is a member of that category; membership requires essential attribution, not mere location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refutation of Multiple Categories – A Fragment
Author begins to refute multiple-category claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's position regarding the possibility of one thing belonging to multiple categories.

Core Idea
The author flatly denies that one thing can belong to multiple categories.

In this fragment, the author begins to refute the claim that a single entity, like north/south or lunch/dinner, can belong to several categories. He calls this belief false. While the argument is incomplete, the fragment establishes his position and invites us to consider the consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The author is responding to the claim that one thing can enter multiple categories (maqulat). Look for the phrase 'الشيء الواحد يدخل في مقولات شتى'.
2
Locate the author's verdict
The author explicitly says 'ظن فاسد' – a false belief. This is a strong dismissal before presenting his reasoning.
3
Examine the examples given
The author mentions 'north and south' (directions) and 'lunch and dinner' (meals). These are cases where something might seem to belong to both a category of relation and a category of time, for instance.
4
Consider why these examples are problematic
North and south are relative directions, and lunch/dinner are temporal events. The author likely argues that they do not have independent essences that would place them in multiple categories simultaneously.
5
Recognize the incomplete argument
The text cuts off with 'وذلك لأن...' (because...). The full reasoning is missing. In later lessons, you will see how the argument continues.
This fragment requires external context for completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم في الشمال والجنوب والتغدي والتعشي، فينبغي أن تعلم أولا أن ظنهم بأن الشيء الواحد يدخل في مقولات شتى ظن فاسد، وذلك لأن
As for their saying concerning north and south and lunch and dinner, you should know first that their belief that one thing falls into multiple categories is a false belief, because...
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a refutation of the claim that one thing (like north/south or lunch/dinner) can belong to multiple categories. The author states that this belief is false and begins to give the reason, but the text cuts off. The passage likely intended to argue that things like directions or times are not independent categories but are relative, and that a single thing cannot have multiple essential categories. Since the argument is incomplete, we must await the continuation.

Key Terms
مقولة
ظن

The author rejects the idea that one thing can belong to multiple categories as false, though his full argument is not given in this fragment.

Distinguishing Existential from Categorical Priority
Author explains the difference between priority of types within a category and priority of kinds of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between priority in existence and priority in the meaning of a category using examples from substance and quantity.

Core Idea
Priority in existence does not imply priority in the genus; they are distinct dimensions.

This passage clarifies a subtle but crucial distinction in the logic of categories: the priority of one thing over another can be existential (causal) or categorical (within the meaning of a genus). Understanding this prevents conflating ontological precedence with logical or definitional precedence. The author uses concrete examples from the categories of substance and quantity to illustrate the point.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of priority
Read the passage and notice that the author contrasts 'priority in existence' (al-taqaddum fi al-wujud) with priority 'by the meaning of the category' (bi-ma'na al-maqulah). Existential priority means one thing causes or is a condition for another's existence. Categorical priority means one species is more fundamental to the definition of the genus.
These are not the same; a cause may be existentially prior but not more generic.
2
Analyze the number example
The author states that three is prior to four in existence (since four depends on three), but not in the meaning of number. Consider: both are equally numbers; three does not define four more than any other number does. So existential priority does not translate to categorical priority.
This example shows the distinction within the category of quantity.
3
Analyze the substance example
Hyle (prime matter) and form are prior to body in existence because they are constituents of body. However, they are not prior in the meaning of substance (jawhariyya) because body is equally a substance. The category 'substance' applies to all equally.
This illustrates the same principle in the category of substance.
4
Understand why 'the existent' is not a genus
The author argues that if one thing's existence causes another's, that does not make the first a genus of the second. Genus must share a common meaning (essence), not just causal relation. Existence is an added meaning, not part of the category's essence.
This principle prevents confusing causality with classification.
5
Apply the principle to a new example
Think of the categories: Substance is prior to accident in existence because substance exists independently. But in the category of accident, an accident like whiteness is not prior to another accident like color in meaning. Check if you can produce your own example from Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جوهرًا موجودًا . وكذلك الثنائية والثلاثية في كونهما عددًا؛ بل كل واحد من المثالين علة لما بعده في الوجود، فقد يكون وجود الشيء علة لوجود شيء آخر، ولذلك لا يكون الموجود جنسا؛ إذ معناه بوجوده الجوهر ثم بتوسطه للعرض. فيتبين إذن أن تُقدم الثلاثة على الأربعة، إنما هو في الوجود وهو غير معنى العدد، وكذلك تُقدم الهيولى والصورة على الجسم في الوجود وهو غير معنى الجوهرية. فبمعنى المقولة إذن إنما تتقدم الأنواع وتتأخر عنها لا لنفسه؛ بل لمعنى يضاف إليه فيه التقديم والتأخير وهو الوجود، فهذا أصل نافع في معرفة الفرق بين تقدم أنواع المقولة بعضها على بعض؛ وبين تقدم أصناف الموجود وما يجري مجراه بعضها على بعض.
Existing as a substance. Likewise, duality and triplicity in being a number; rather each of the two examples is a cause for what comes after it in existence, for the existence of a thing may be a cause for the existence of another thing, and therefore 'the existent' is not a genus; because its meaning is by its existence the substance, then by its intermediation for the accident. So it becomes clear that the priority of three over four is only in existence, and that is not the meaning of number; likewise, the priority of hyle and form over body is in existence, and that is not the meaning of substantiality. So by the meaning of the category, the types are prior and posterior not by themselves, but by a meaning added to them in which there is priority and posteriority, which is existence. So this is a useful principle for knowing the difference between the priority of some types of a category over others, and the priority of some kinds of the existent and what runs its course over others.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological clarification distinguishing two kinds of priority: priority in existence and priority in the meaning of a category. The author begins by noting that the existence of a thing can be a cause for another thing's existence, but this existential causality does not make 'the existent' a genus. For example, the number three is prior to four in existence (since four depends on three), but not in the meaning of number. Similarly, hyle (prime matter) and form are prior to body in existence, but not in the meaning of substantiality. The key point is that the priority of types within a category is due to an added meaning (existence), not to the category itself. This principle helps differentiate between the relative priority of species within a category (like number or substance) and the priority of things in existence (like causes over effects). The discussion uses examples from Aristotelian metaphysics but remains within the Islamic logical tradition of analyzing categories.

Checkpoint
Which of the following best illustrates the distinction between priority in existence and priority in the meaning of a category?
A) The number three is prior to four in the genus of quantity because three is simpler.
B) Hyle and form are prior to body in existence, but not in the category of substance.
C) A substance is prior to its accidents in the category of substance.
D) A cause is prior to its effect in both existence and the category of relation.

Key Takeaway
Priority in existence does not imply priority in the meaning of a category; they are separate dimensions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following: In the category of relation, fatherhood is prior to sonship in existence (since father exists before son). Is fatherhood also prior in the meaning of the category? Explain using the principle from this passage.
?
In kalam, al-Ash'ari argues that the attributes of God are neither identical to His essence nor separate. Could the principle of distinguishing existential from categorical priority help clarify his position? Draft a short analysis.
