The Definition of Substance: Essence vs. Actual Existence
Author clarifies definition of substance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the definition of substance based on essence (mahiyya) and that based on actual existence in external reality.

Core Idea
Substance is defined by its essential mode, not by existing actually.

In this passage, the author tackles a key confusion: does the definition of substance as 'existent not in a subject' require that a substance actually exists in the external world? He argues no—the definition describes the essence (mahiyya) and how it would exist if realized externally. This clarification saves universals and absent substances from being excluded, and it allows substance to function as a true genus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard definition
The author states that substance is defined as 'existent not in a subject' (al-mawjud la fi mawdu'). Write down this definition and note that it appears in many logic textbooks.
This is a rasm taam (complete description).
2
Spot the problem with universals
The author points out that if 'existent' meant actual existence, then universals (kulliyyat) like 'humanity' could not be substances, because they only exist in the mind (a subject). Why would that be a problem?
Universals are considered substances in the Aristotelian-Islamic ontology.
3
Understand the author's solution
The author clarifies that 'existent' in the definition refers to the essence (mahiyya): it means that if the thing were to exist externally, its mode of existence would be 'not in a subject'. This is like calling someone 'laughing' because of their disposition, not current action.
The definition is about essential mode, not actual existence.
4
Apply with the example of Zayd
The author says: you know Zayd is a substance even when he is absent (non-existent to you). You know his essence (mahiyya) includes the property of existing not in a subject if he were to exist. So the definition holds without requiring his actual existence.
This shows that actual existence is an accident (lahiq) not a constituent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الجوهر ] أما لفظة الجوهر فزعم قوم أنه إن أريد إطلاقها على الأجسام وحدها أمكن أن يقال عليها بالتواطؤ قولا جنسيا، فأما إذا أريد إطلاقها على ما هو الأعم منها فذلك إنما يقال بالاتفاق والتشكيك قول الوجود. وذلك لأن الهيولى والصورة أقدم في معنى الجوهرية من المركب والمفارق الذي هو سبب وجودهما وسبب قوام أحدهما بالأخرى؛ وهو أقدم من جميع ذلك، وإن المبادئ لا تقع مع ذوات المبادئ في مقولة واحدة. ومع ذلك فقد اعترفوا بأن كونها موجودة لا في موضوع أمر يشترك فيها جميعها، وإن كان الموجود لا في موضوع لبعضها قبل بعض. وقالوا إن الوجود إذا كان يقال على هذه الجملة بالتقدم والتأخر بلحوق لا في موضوع به من بعد، وهو معنى سلبي ليس يجعل الوجود فيها على مرتبة واحدة. فنقول إن من هذه الجهات لا يلزم أن لا يكون الجوهر جنسًا، وكيف والأجسام المشتركة في جنس الجسم ليست في مرتبة بل بعضها أقدم وأما حديث الموجود في رسم الجوهر ففيه من الكلام: فإن قولنا إن الجوهر هو الموجود لا في موضوع ليس يُعنى بالموجود فيه الموجود من حيث هو الموجود وإلا لا يمكن أن تكون الكليات جواهر، وإنها من جملة ما لا وجود لها في الأعيان البتة، وأما وجودها في النفس فكوجود شيء في موضوع. وإن عني بالموجود هو الموجود في الأعيان كان الأمر بالحقيقة على ما يذهبون إليه، وكان بعضها قبل بعض فيه بل يعنون بالموجود لا في موضوع المعنى الذي يلزمه في الأعيان إذا وجد أن يكون وجوده لا في موضوع مثل ما يقال ضاحك أي من بيانه عند العجب أن يضحك. ولو تأملت في زيد إذا غاب عنك فقد ظهر عليك ما يكون معنى الجوهر وما لا يكون : فإنك تعلم أنه ماهية إذا كانت موجودة في الأعيان كانت لا في موضوع، وتعلم أن هذا المعنى هو المقوم الأول لحقيقته كما تعلم أنه جوهر، ولا تعلم هو موجود في الأعيان بالفعل لا في موضوع بل ربما كان عندك معدومًا. فقد بان أن الوجود بالفعل في الأعيان لا في موضوع ليس مقوما لماهية زيد ولا لشيء من الجوهر بل هو أمر يلحق لحوق الوجود الذي هو لاحق لماهية الأشياء؛ فليس هذا جنسا بل الأول. ولذلك إذا كان شيء ماهيته هي الوجود وكان منزها عن الموضوع لم يكن في جنس ولا يشارك الجوهر بمعنى أنه شيء يلحقه الوجود إذا لحق بهذه الصفة بل لا يوجد أمر مقوم لذلك الشيء ولنوعيات الجواهر بالشركة. فإن ما هو ذاتي لذلك الشيء فنظيره عرضي لهذه كالوجود الحاصل كيف كان. وما هو ذاتي لهذه النوعيات فذلك غير مقول على ذلك الشيء فإنه ليس هناك ماهية غير الوجود. فقد ظهر حقيقة وصف الجوهر بصفة أنه موجود لا في موضوع وإن كونه بهذه الصفة أمر لا تقدم فيه ولا تأخر، ولولا أن حقيقة الجوهرية التي أوضحناها لا تقدم فيها ولا تأخر. فلا يمكنك أن تقول إن الصورة في نفسها هي ماهية إذا وجدت في الأعيان لم يفتقر إلى موضوع؛ وإن هذه الحقيقة للمركب في أنها بهذه الصفة متعلقة فتكون الصورة على هذه الصفة فإذن هذا غير موجب أن لا يكون الجوهر جنسًا؛ وهذا هو معنى ذات الجوهر.
Chapter: On Substance. As for the word 'substance', some people claim that if it is intended to be applied to bodies alone, it can be said of them by univocity in a generic sense. But if it is intended to be applied to what is more general than them, then that is only said by agreement and equivocation as the word 'existence'. That is because matter and form are prior in the meaning of substantiality to the composite and the separate [intellect] which is the cause of their existence and the cause of the subsistence of one by the other; and it is prior to all that, and the principles do not fall with the things having principles in one category. Nevertheless, they acknowledged that their being existent not in a subject is a matter in which all of them share, even if the 'existent not in a subject' is for some of them before others. And they said that if existence is said of this whole with priority and posteriority by the accident of 'not in a subject' afterwards, and it is a negative meaning, it does not make existence in them on one level. We say that from these aspects it does not necessitate that substance is not a genus. How so, when bodies that share in the genus of body are not on one level but some are prior? As for the talk about 'the existent' in the definition of substance, there is discussion in it: Our saying that substance is the existent not in a subject does not mean by 'the existent' the existent insofar as it is existent, otherwise universals could not be substances, and they are among those that have no existence in external entities at all; rather their existence in the soul is like the existence of something in a subject. And if by 'the existent' is meant the existent in external entities, then the matter is in reality as they hold, and some of them are before others in it. Rather they mean by 'existent not in a subject' the meaning that necessarily accompanies it in external entities when it exists, that its existence is not in a subject, like it is said 'laughing' meaning one whose characteristic when astonished is to laugh. And if you reflect on Zayd when he is absent from you, it becomes clear to you what the meaning of substance is and what it is not: You know that he is an essence (mahiyya) that, if it were existent in external entities, would be not in a subject, and you know that this meaning is the first constituent of his reality just as you know that he is a substance, and you do not know that he is actually existent in external entities not in a subject, rather perhaps he is non-existent for you. So it is clear that actual existence in external entities not in a subject is not a constituent of the essence of Zayd nor of any substance, rather it is something that attaches as an attachment of existence which is consequent upon the essences of things; so this [actual existence] is not a genus, but the first [meaning] is. Therefore, if something has its essence as existence and is free from subject, it is not in a genus and does not share with substance in the sense that existence attaches to it when it attaches with this attribute; rather there is no constitutive thing for that thing and for the species of substance by sharing. For what is essential to that thing, its counterpart is accidental to these [species], like existence however it occurs. And what is essential to these species is not predicated of that thing, for there is no essence other than existence. So the reality of describing substance with the attribute that it is existent not in a subject has become clear, and that its being with this attribute is a matter with no priority or posteriority. Were it not that the reality of substantiality which we have explained has no priority or posteriority. So you cannot say that form in itself is an essence that when it exists in external entities does not need a subject; and that this reality for the composite in that it is with this attribute is dependent, so form is with this attribute. Therefore, this does not necessitate that substance is not a genus. And this is the meaning of the essence of substance.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the category of substance (jawhar). The author begins by addressing the objection that substance cannot be a genus because its instances are not all on the same level (some are prior, like matter and form). He argues that this does not entail that substance is not a genus, just as bodies share in the genus 'body' despite some being prior. The key issue is the definition of substance as 'existent not in a subject' (al-mawjud la fi mawdu‘). The author clarifies that this definition does not refer to actual existence in external reality; otherwise, universals (kulliyyat) could not be substances, as they exist only in the mind, which is a subject. Rather, the definition refers to the essence (mahiyya) of a substance: if it were to exist externally, its mode of existence would be not in a subject. This is analogous to saying someone is 'laughing' not because they are actually laughing but because they have the disposition to laugh. The author uses the example of Zayd: we know his essence as a substance even when he is absent (non-existent). Thus actual existence is not constitutive of substantiality; it is an accident (lahiq). This allows substance to be a genus encompassing all its species despite differences in priority and actual existence. The passage also touches on the case of something whose essence is existence itself (i.e., God), which is not in a genus. All examples are rooted in Islamic ontological discussions.

Key Terms
الجوهر
الموجود لا في موضوع
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the definition of substance as 'existent not in a subject' not prevent universals from being substances?
A) Because universals actually exist in the external world.
B) Because 'existent' in the definition refers to the essence, not actual existence.
C) Because universals are not substances.
D) Because the definition only applies to bodies.

Key Takeaway
The definition of substance describes the essence (mahiyya) and its characteristic mode, not actual existence; this allows substance to be a genus encompassing all instances regardless of existential status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Task: Consider the universal 'humanity'. According to the passage, is 'humanity' a substance? Why or why not? Provide your reasoning in terms of essence vs. actual existence.

Why Substance is a Genus Despite Varying Priority
Author defends substance as a genus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend that substance can be a genus even though its instances differ in priority and existential status.

Core Idea
Substance is a genus like 'body' despite variations in priority among species.

A common objection against substance being a genus is that its instances (matter, form, composite, separate intellect) are not all on the same level—some are ontologically prior. The author counters that this does not negate generic unity, just as bodies share the genus 'body' despite some bodies (e.g., celestial bodies) being prior to others. In this lesson, we will see why the shared attribute 'existent not in a subject' suffices for generic participation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The author states that some people argue substance cannot be a genus because matter and form are prior in substantiality to the composite and the separate intellect. Principles (mabadi) do not fall in the same category as their derivative things.
The objection is that generic unity requires equality in all attributes, which is not the case here.
2
Find the author's analogy
The author counters: bodies share the genus 'body' even though some bodies (like celestial spheres) are ontologically prior to others (like earthly bodies). So priority does not preclude generic unity.
This is a key distinction: sharing a genus does not require equality in priority.
3
Analyze the shared attribute
The shared attribute among substances is 'existing not in a subject' (al-mawjud la fi mawdu'). The author emphasizes that this attribute is common to all, even if some are prior in the mode of being 'not in a subject'. The negativity of the attribute does not create different levels of existence.
The attribute is a negative description, but it is sufficient for generic unity.
4
Apply the reasoning to a new case
Consider the genus 'body': it includes celestial bodies (which are eternal, not subject to generation) and earthly bodies (which are temporal). Despite their differences in priority and properties, they still fall under one genus. Similarly, substance includes different species with varying priority.
This demonstrates that generic unity is based on a common essential attribute, not on equal standing in all respects.

Division of Substance: Simple and Composite
Author divides substance into simple and composite and defines matter and form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any substance as simple or composite based on its composition, and distinguish matter from form as simple constituents.

Core Idea
Substance divides into simple (not composed) and composite (composed of substances); matter and form are simple substances.

This passage introduces a foundational division in ontology: the classification of substances into simple and composite. Simple substances are not made of other substances, while composites are. The author uses the concrete example of a chair to illustrate how matter (wood) and form (shape) are both simple substances that together constitute a composite. Understanding this division is crucial for analyzing the composition of physical objects in Islamic natural philosophy and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main division
Find the sentence that explicitly divides substance (jawhar) into two categories. What are the two categories called in Arabic?
The text begins: 'ثم الجوهر إما بسيط وإما مركب'.
2
Extract the definition of simple substance
Locate the definition of 'بسيط' (simple). Note the condition that it is not composed of things each of which is a substance. What examples are given?
Definition: 'الذي لا يركب من أشياء كل واحد منها جوهر'.
3
Extract the definition of composite substance
Find the definition of 'مركب' (composite). How does it differ from simple?
Definition: 'ما يركب من أشياء كل واحد منها جوهر'.
4
Distinguish matter and form
The passage subdivides simple substances that enter composites into two types: matter (المادة) and form (الصورة). Using the chair example, identify which component corresponds to which role.
Wood is matter, shape is form.
5
Check completeness of definitions
Verify whether the definitions include all instances and exclude non-instances. Are there any substances that are neither simple nor composite according to these definitions? What about accidents?
Accidents are not substances, so they are not covered. The definitions are exclusive and exhaustive for substances.

ثم الجوهر إما بسيط وإما مركب؛ والبسيط هو الذي لا يركب من أشياء كل واحد منها جوهر نحو المادة والصورة، والمركب ما يركب من أشياء كل واحد منها جوهر والبسيط إما أن يكون داخلا في تقويم المركب وإما أن لا يكون والداخل فيه إما هو الداخل كالخشب في الكرسي وهو المادة، أو كشكل الكرسي في الوجود وهو الصورة والمادة هي ما لا يكون باعتباره
Then substance is either simple or composite. The simple is that which is not composed of things each of which is a substance, such as matter and form. The composite is what is composed of things each of which is a substance. The simple is either entering into the constitution of the composite or not. That which enters is either the entering [component] like wood in the chair, and it is matter, or like the shape of the chair in existence, and it is form. And matter is that which is not considered in its...
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage provides a definitional division of substance (jawhar) into two main categories: simple (basit) and composite (murakkab). Simple substance is defined as that which is not composed of other substances; examples are matter (madda) and form (sura). Composite substance is composed of simpler substances, each of which is a substance. The author further distinguishes two kinds of simple substance that enter into the constitution of composites: one is the material component (like wood in a chair), which is matter, and the other is the formal component (like the shape of the chair), which is form. Matter is described as that which is not considered in its... (the text cuts off). This division is foundational in Aristotelian physics as adopted by Islamic philosophers. In an Islamic context, the example of a chair (kursi) is used to illustrate the distinction: the wood (matter) and the shape (form) both are simple substances that together constitute the composite substance (the chair). The text emphasizes that both matter and form are themselves substances, not accidents.

Definition of Jawhar: Substance as Not-in-a-Subject
Definition of substance (jawhar) and clarification that existence is accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define jawhar as a quiddity whose nature is not to be in a subject, and distinguish this from its actual existence.

Core Idea
Jawhar is defined by potential not needing a subject, not by actual existence.

In this passage, the author clarifies the fundamental meaning of jawhar. It is often misunderstood as requiring actual existence independently, but the author shows that existence is accidental to its quiddity. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly categorizing substances in Islamic logic and metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition
Locate where the author defines what jawhar is: 'a quiddity which, if it exists in external entities, is not in a subject.' Notice that the definition is conditional ('if it exists').
2
Separate quiddity from existence
The author then argues that actual existence not in a subject is not part of the quiddity of any substance. It is an accident that attaches after. Identify the sentence where he says this is not a genus but the first.
The first genus of categories is jawhar itself, not existence.
3
Understand the consequence
The author draws a conclusion: if something's quiddity is existence (e.g., the Necessary Being), it is not under the genus of substance. Recognize why this distinction matters for the categories.
This shows that existence is broader than substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد ظهر عليك ما يكون معنى الجوهر وما لا يكون : فإنك تعلم أنه ماهية إذا كانت موجودة في الأعيان كانت لا في موضوع، وتعلم أن هذا المعنى هو المقوم الأول لحقيقته كما تعلم أنه جوهر، ولا تعلم هو موجود في الأعيان بالفعل لا في موضوع بل ربما كان عندك معدومًا. فقد بان أن الوجود بالفعل في الأعيان لا في موضوع ليس مقوما لماهية زيد ولا لشيء من الجوهر بل هو أمر يلحق لحوق الوجود الذي هو لاحق لماهية الأشياء؛ فليس هذا جنسا بل الأول. ولذلك إذا كان شيء ماهيته هي الوجود وكان منزها عن الموضوع لم يكن في جنس ولا يشارك الجوهر بمعنى أنه شيء يلحقه الوجود إذا لحق بهذه الصفة بل لا يوجد أمر مقوم لذلك الشيء ولنوعيات الجواهر بالشركة. فإن ما هو ذاتي لذلك الشيء فنظيره عرضي لهذه كالوجود الحاصل كيف كان. وما هو ذاتي لهذه النوعيات فذلك غير مقول على ذلك الشيء فإنه ليس هناك ماهية غير الوجود. فقد ظهر حقيقة وصف الجوهر بصفة أنه موجود لا في موضوع وإن كونه بهذه الصفة أمر لا تقدم فيه ولا تأخر، ولولا أن حقيقة الجوهرية التي أوضحناها لا تقدم فيها ولا تأخر. فلا يمكنك أن تقول إن الصورة في نفسها هي ماهية إذا وجدت في الأعيان لم يفتقر إلى موضوع؛ وإن هذه الحقيقة للمركب في أنها بهذه الصفة متعلقة فتكون الصورة على هذه الصفة فإذن هذا غير موجب أن لا يكون الجوهر جنسًا ; وهذا هو معنى ذات الجوهر. ثم الجوهر إما بسيط وإما مركب ; والبسيط هو الذي لا يركب من أشياء كل واحد منها جوهر نحو المادة والصورة، والمركب ما يركب من أشياء كل واحد منها جوهر والبسيط إما أن يكون داخلا في تقويم المركب وإما أن لا يكون والداخل فيه إما هو الداخل كالخشب في الكرسي وهو المادة، أو كشكل الكرسي في الوجود وهو الصورة والمادة هي ما لا يكون باعتباره وحده للمركب وجود بالفعل بل بالقوة، والصورة هي ما بها يصير المركب هو ما هو بالفعل; وجميع ذلك إما أن يوجد كليا، وإما أن يوجد جزئيا. ولما كان المعنى بالجوهر هو الشيء الذي من حق وجود الماهية له في الأعيان أن تكون لا في موضوع وجب أن تكون ماهية الإنسان بحقيقتها جوهرا، فالإنسان مثلا إنما هو جوهر ; لأنه إنسان لا لأنه موجود في الأعيان نحوا من الوجود، وإنه إذا كان جوهرًا; لأنه إنسان مما لحقه من بعد; فذلك أمر يلحق جوهرًا. ولواحق الجوهر لوازم وأعراض لا تبطل معها جوهريته، فالأشخاص إذن في الأعيان جواهر; فما يكون من المعقولات الكلية فذلك أيضًا جوهر وإلا لما صح عليه أنه ماهية حقها في الوجود أن لا يكون في الموضوع; والمشارك للجوهر بماهية جوهر لا محالة. ولذلك فإن حد النوع من حيث هو طبيعة وحد للجنس أيضًا من حيث هو طبيعة ومحمولان على الأشخاص، فهي إذن جواهر; وما يشاركها في حدها فكذلك . ولو كانت جوهريتها لما أنها موجودة في الأعيان فكيفيته بالأعراض لكانت جوهرية الأمور عارضة لماهيتها; إذ صح أن الوجود عارض في هذه الماهيات ولكانت العوارض تجعل ما ليس في نفسه بجوهر جوهرًا فيكون شيء عرض له إن كان جوهرا، فتكون الجوهرية عارضة لشيء; وذلك محال فكليات الجواهر جواهر في ماهياتها.
It has become clear to you what is the meaning of substance and what is not: for you know that it is a quiddity which, if it exists in external entities, is not in a subject, and you know that this meaning is the first constituent of its reality just as you know that it is a substance, and you do not know whether it exists in external entities actually not in a subject, but perhaps it is non-existent to you. Thus it has become evident that actual existence in external entities not in a subject is not constitutive of the quiddity of Zayd nor of any substance, but rather it is an accident that attaches like existence attaches, which is accidental to the quiddity of things; so this is not a genus but the first [genus]. Therefore if something has existence as its quiddity and is free from subjecthood, it would not be under a genus and would not share with substance in the sense that something to which existence attaches with this attribute, but no constitutive thing exists for that thing and for the specific types of substances by commonality. For what is essential to that thing is analogous to what is accidental to these, like existence as it occurs. And what is essential to these types is not predicated of that thing, for there is no quiddity other than existence. Thus the reality of describing substance by the attribute that it is existent not in a subject has become clear, and that being with this attribute is a matter having no priority or posteriority; and were it not that the reality of substantiality which we have clarified has no priority or posteriority. So you cannot say that form in itself is a quiddity which when it exists in external entities does not need a subject; and that this reality for the composite in that it is with this attribute is attached, so that form is upon this attribute. Then this does not necessitate that substance is not a genus; and this is the meaning of the essence of substance. Then substance is either simple or composite; the simple is that which is not composed of things each of which is a substance, such as matter and form; the composite is that which is composed of things each of which is a substance. The simple is either entering into the constitution of the composite or not; what enters is either like wood in a chair, which is matter, or like the shape of the chair in existence, which is form. Matter is that by whose consideration alone the composite does not have actual existence but only potentiality; form is that by which the composite becomes what it is actually. All of this is either found universally or found particularly. Since the meaning of substance is that thing of which it is the right that its quiddity exists in external entities not in a subject, it is necessary that the quiddity of man in its reality be a substance. For man is a substance because he is man, not because he exists in external entities in a certain mode of existence; and if he is a substance because he is man, that is something that attaches to him afterwards; so that is an accident that attaches to substance. The concomitants of substance are necessary accompaniments and accidents that do not nullify its substantiality. So individuals in external entities are substances; whatever is universal intelligible is also a substance, otherwise it would not be valid that it is a quiddity whose right in existence is not to be in a subject; and what shares with substance in quiddity is undoubtedly a substance. Therefore the definition of species as it is a nature is a definition of the genus also as it is a nature, and they are predicated of individuals, so they are substances; and what shares with them in their definition is likewise. If their substantiality were because they exist in external entities, then their accidental quality would make the substantiality of things accidental to their quiddity; since it is valid that existence is accidental to these quiddities, and accidents would make that which is not in itself a substance become a substance, so something would have accidental that it is a substance; and that is impossible. So universals of substances are substances in their quiddities.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage defines the meaning of substance (jawhar) and clarifies its essential nature. The author begins by affirming that substance is a quiddity which, when existent in the external world, is not in a subject—meaning it subsists by itself, not inhering in something else. This is the primary constitutive meaning of substantiality. The author emphasizes that actual existence in the external world is not part of the quiddity of substance; rather, existence is an accident (lahaq) that attaches to the quiddity. Thus, substance as a genus is defined by the potentiality of being not-in-a-subject, not by actual existence. The author then classifies substance into simple (basit) and composite (murakkab). Simple substance is not composed of other substances (e.g., matter and form). Composite substance is composed of such substances. Within composites, matter (madda) is that by which the composite has potential existence, and form (sura) is that by which it has actual existence. Examples are given: wood is matter for a chair, its shape is form. The author then argues that both individuals (juz'iyyat) and universals (kulliyyat) are substances. The quiddity of man is a substance because of what it is in itself, not because of its existence. If substantiality were due to existence, then universal concepts would not be substances, but the definition of species and genus predicated on individuals shows they share the same quiddity, so they must be substances. The passage concludes that universals of substances are indeed substances in their quiddities, as denying this would lead to absurdity.

Key Terms
الجوهر
ماهية
موضوع
عرض

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of jawhar?
A) Something that exists independently in the external world.
B) A quiddity which, if it exists in external entities, is not in a subject.
C) A quiddity that has actual existence not in a subject.
D) The first genus of categories, defined by actual existence.

Key Takeaway
Jawhar is defined by its nature not to need a subject, not by actually existing without one.

Division of Substance into Simple and Composite
Division of substance into simple and composite, with explanation of matter and form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between simple (basit) and composite (murakkab) substances, and identify the roles of matter (madda) and form (sura) in composites.

Core Idea
Substances are either simple (not composed of other substances) or composite (composed of substances, with matter as potentiality and form as actuality).

Having clarified the definition of substance, the author now divides it into two types: simple and composite. Simple substances are those not composed of other substances, such as matter and form considered in themselves. Composite substances are those composed of multiple substances, and they have two components: matter (potentiality) and form (actuality). Understanding this division is essential for analyzing physical substances in Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dichotomy
The author states: 'Then substance is either simple or composite.' Locate this and note the definitions.
2
Define simple substance
Simple substance is not composed of things each of which is a substance. Examples given: matter and form considered alone.
Matter and form are simple substances, but they combine to form composites.
3
Define composite and its components
Composite is composed of simple substances. The author then explains that within composites, matter gives potential existence, form gives actual existence. Use the example of a chair: wood is matter, shape is form.

It has become clear to you what is the meaning of substance and what is not: for you know that it is a quiddity which, if it exists in external entities, is not in a subject, and you know that this meaning is the first constituent of its reality just as you know that it is a substance, and you do not know whether it exists in external entities actually not in a subject, but perhaps it is non-existent to you. Thus it has become evident that actual existence in external entities not in a subject is not constitutive of the quiddity of Zayd nor of any substance, but rather it is an accident that attaches like existence attaches, which is accidental to the quiddity of things; so this is not a genus but the first [genus]. Therefore if something has existence as its quiddity and is free from subjecthood, it would not be under a genus and would not share with substance in the sense that something to which existence attaches with this attribute, but no constitutive thing exists for that thing and for the specific types of substances by commonality. For what is essential to that thing is analogous to what is accidental to these, like existence as it occurs. And what is essential to these types is not predicated of that thing, for there is no quiddity other than existence. Thus the reality of describing substance by the attribute that it is existent not in a subject has become clear, and that being with this attribute is a matter having no priority or posteriority; and were it not that the reality of substantiality which we have clarified has no priority or posteriority. So you cannot say that form in itself is a quiddity which when it exists in external entities does not need a subject; and that this reality for the composite in that it is with this attribute is attached, so that form is upon this attribute. Then this does not necessitate that substance is not a genus; and this is the meaning of the essence of substance. Then substance is either simple or composite; the simple is that which is not composed of things each of which is a substance, such as matter and form; the composite is that which is composed of things each of which is a substance. The simple is either entering into the constitution of the composite or not; what enters is either like wood in a chair, which is matter, or like the shape of the chair in existence, which is form. Matter is that by whose consideration alone the composite does not have actual existence but only potentiality; form is that by which the composite becomes what it is actually. All of this is either found universally or found particularly. Since the meaning of substance is that thing of which it is the right that its quiddity exists in external entities not in a subject, it is necessary that the quiddity of man in its reality be a substance. For man is a substance because he is man, not because he exists in external entities in a certain mode of existence; and if he is a substance because he is man, that is something that attaches to him afterwards; so that is an accident that attaches to substance. The concomitants of substance are necessary accompaniments and accidents that do not nullify its substantiality. So individuals in external entities are substances; whatever is universal intelligible is also a substance, otherwise it would not be valid that it is a quiddity whose right in existence is not to be in a subject; and what shares with substance in quiddity is undoubtedly a substance. Therefore the definition of species as it is a nature is a definition of the genus also as it is a nature, and they are predicated of individuals, so they are substances; and what shares with them in their definition is likewise. If their substantiality were because they exist in external entities, then their accidental quality would make the substantiality of things accidental to their quiddity; since it is valid that existence is accidental to these quiddities, and accidents would make that which is not in itself a substance become a substance, so something would have accidental that it is a substance; and that is impossible. So universals of substances are substances in their quiddities.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage defines the meaning of substance (jawhar) and clarifies its essential nature. The author begins by affirming that substance is a quiddity which, when existent in the external world, is not in a subject—meaning it subsists by itself, not inhering in something else. This is the primary constitutive meaning of substantiality. The author emphasizes that actual existence in the external world is not part of the quiddity of substance; rather, existence is an accident (lahaq) that attaches to the quiddity. Thus, substance as a genus is defined by the potentiality of being not-in-a-subject, not by actual existence. The author then classifies substance into simple (basit) and composite (murakkab). Simple substance is not composed of other substances (e.g., matter and form). Composite substance is composed of such substances. Within composites, matter (madda) is that by which the composite has potential existence, and form (sura) is that by which it has actual existence. Examples are given: wood is matter for a chair, its shape is form. The author then argues that both individuals (juz'iyyat) and universals (kulliyyat) are substances. The quiddity of man is a substance because of what it is in itself, not because of its existence. If substantiality were due to existence, then universal concepts would not be substances, but the definition of species and genus predicated on individuals shows they share the same quiddity, so they must be substances. The passage concludes that universals of substances are indeed substances in their quiddities, as denying this would lead to absurdity.

Universals as Substances: The Argument from Quiddity
Argument that universals are substances in their quiddities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that universals (kulliyyat) are substances in their quiddities, and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Universals are substances because their quiddity requires not being in a subject, just like individuals.

After defining substance and classifying it, the author argues that universals (e.g., the concept 'human') are also substances. This might seem counterintuitive because universals do not exist in the external world as such. However, the author shows that substantiality is based on the nature (quiddity) of the concept, not its mode of existence. This argument has important implications for the ontology of universals in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The author's conclusion is that universals of substances are substances in their quiddities. Find the sentence in the Arabic that states this.
2
Find the premises
The reasoning: The meaning of substance is that its quiddity, if existent, is not in a subject. The quiddity of man is such regardless of existence. Therefore, the universal 'man' is a substance.
3
Understand the reductio
The author uses a reductio: if substantiality were due to existence, then accidents would make non-substances into substances, which is absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد ظهر عليك ما يكون معنى الجوهر وما لا يكون : فإنك تعلم أنه ماهية إذا كانت موجودة في الأعيان كانت لا في موضوع، وتعلم أن هذا المعنى هو المقوم الأول لحقيقته كما تعلم أنه جوهر، ولا تعلم هو موجود في الأعيان بالفعل لا في موضوع بل ربما كان عندك معدومًا. فقد بان أن الوجود بالفعل في الأعيان لا في موضوع ليس مقوما لماهية زيد ولا لشيء من الجوهر بل هو أمر يلحق لحوق الوجود الذي هو لاحق لماهية الأشياء؛ فليس هذا جنسا بل الأول. ولذلك إذا كان شيء ماهيته هي الوجود وكان منزها عن الموضوع لم يكن في جنس ولا يشارك الجوهر بمعنى أنه شيء يلحقه الوجود إذا لحق بهذه الصفة بل لا يوجد أمر مقوم لذلك الشيء ولنوعيات الجواهر بالشركة. فإن ما هو ذاتي لذلك الشيء فنظيره عرضي لهذه كالوجود الحاصل كيف كان. وما هو ذاتي لهذه النوعيات فذلك غير مقول على ذلك الشيء فإنه ليس هناك ماهية غير الوجود. فقد ظهر حقيقة وصف الجوهر بصفة أنه موجود لا في موضوع وإن كونه بهذه الصفة أمر لا تقدم فيه ولا تأخر، ولولا أن حقيقة الجوهرية التي أوضحناها لا تقدم فيها ولا تأخر. فلا يمكنك أن تقول إن الصورة في نفسها هي ماهية إذا وجدت في الأعيان لم يفتقر إلى موضوع؛ وإن هذه الحقيقة للمركب في أنها بهذه الصفة متعلقة فتكون الصورة على هذه الصفة فإذن هذا غير موجب أن لا يكون الجوهر جنسًا ; وهذا هو معنى ذات الجوهر. ثم الجوهر إما بسيط وإما مركب ; والبسيط هو الذي لا يركب من أشياء كل واحد منها جوهر نحو المادة والصورة، والمركب ما يركب من أشياء كل واحد منها جوهر والبسيط إما أن يكون داخلا في تقويم المركب وإما أن لا يكون والداخل فيه إما هو الداخل كالخشب في الكرسي وهو المادة، أو كشكل الكرسي في الوجود وهو الصورة والمادة هي ما لا يكون باعتباره وحده للمركب وجود بالفعل بل بالقوة، والصورة هي ما بها يصير المركب هو ما هو بالفعل; وجميع ذلك إما أن يوجد كليا، وإما أن يوجد جزئيا. ولما كان المعنى بالجوهر هو الشيء الذي من حق وجود الماهية له في الأعيان أن تكون لا في موضوع وجب أن تكون ماهية الإنسان بحقيقتها جوهرا، فالإنسان مثلا إنما هو جوهر ; لأنه إنسان لا لأنه موجود في الأعيان نحوا من الوجود، وإنه إذا كان جوهرًا; لأنه إنسان مما لحقه من بعد; فذلك أمر يلحق جوهرًا. ولواحق الجوهر لوازم وأعراض لا تبطل معها جوهريته، فالأشخاص إذن في الأعيان جواهر; فما يكون من المعقولات الكلية فذلك أيضًا جوهر وإلا لما صح عليه أنه ماهية حقها في الوجود أن لا يكون في الموضوع; والمشارك للجوهر بماهية جوهر لا محالة. ولذلك فإن حد النوع من حيث هو طبيعة وحد للجنس أيضًا من حيث هو طبيعة ومحمولان على الأشخاص، فهي إذن جواهر; وما يشاركها في حدها فكذلك . ولو كانت جوهريتها لما أنها موجودة في الأعيان فكيفيته بالأعراض لكانت جوهرية الأمور عارضة لماهيتها; إذ صح أن الوجود عارض في هذه الماهيات ولكانت العوارض تجعل ما ليس في نفسه بجوهر جوهرًا فيكون شيء عرض له إن كان جوهرا، فتكون الجوهرية عارضة لشيء; وذلك محال فكليات الجواهر جواهر في ماهياتها.
It has become clear to you what is the meaning of substance and what is not: for you know that it is a quiddity which, if it exists in external entities, is not in a subject, and you know that this meaning is the first constituent of its reality just as you know that it is a substance, and you do not know whether it exists in external entities actually not in a subject, but perhaps it is non-existent to you. Thus it has become evident that actual existence in external entities not in a subject is not constitutive of the quiddity of Zayd nor of any substance, but rather it is an accident that attaches like existence attaches, which is accidental to the quiddity of things; so this is not a genus but the first [genus]. Therefore if something has existence as its quiddity and is free from subjecthood, it would not be under a genus and would not share with substance in the sense that something to which existence attaches with this attribute, but no constitutive thing exists for that thing and for the specific types of substances by commonality. For what is essential to that thing is analogous to what is accidental to these, like existence as it occurs. And what is essential to these types is not predicated of that thing, for there is no quiddity other than existence. Thus the reality of describing substance by the attribute that it is existent not in a subject has become clear, and that being with this attribute is a matter having no priority or posteriority; and were it not that the reality of substantiality which we have clarified has no priority or posteriority. So you cannot say that form in itself is a quiddity which when it exists in external entities does not need a subject; and that this reality for the composite in that it is with this attribute is attached, so that form is upon this attribute. Then this does not necessitate that substance is not a genus; and this is the meaning of the essence of substance. Then substance is either simple or composite; the simple is that which is not composed of things each of which is a substance, such as matter and form; the composite is that which is composed of things each of which is a substance. The simple is either entering into the constitution of the composite or not; what enters is either like wood in a chair, which is matter, or like the shape of the chair in existence, which is form. Matter is that by whose consideration alone the composite does not have actual existence but only potentiality; form is that by which the composite becomes what it is actually. All of this is either found universally or found particularly. Since the meaning of substance is that thing of which it is the right that its quiddity exists in external entities not in a subject, it is necessary that the quiddity of man in its reality be a substance. For man is a substance because he is man, not because he exists in external entities in a certain mode of existence; and if he is a substance because he is man, that is something that attaches to him afterwards; so that is an accident that attaches to substance. The concomitants of substance are necessary accompaniments and accidents that do not nullify its substantiality. So individuals in external entities are substances; whatever is universal intelligible is also a substance, otherwise it would not be valid that it is a quiddity whose right in existence is not to be in a subject; and what shares with substance in quiddity is undoubtedly a substance. Therefore the definition of species as it is a nature is a definition of the genus also as it is a nature, and they are predicated of individuals, so they are substances; and what shares with them in their definition is likewise. If their substantiality were because they exist in external entities, then their accidental quality would make the substantiality of things accidental to their quiddity; since it is valid that existence is accidental to these quiddities, and accidents would make that which is not in itself a substance become a substance, so something would have accidental that it is a substance; and that is impossible. So universals of substances are substances in their quiddities.

Ranks of Substances and the Two Senses of 'First'
Author introduces the ranks of substances and distinguishes two senses of 'first'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two senses of 'first' in the classification of substances and identify which sense applies to individual substances.

Core Idea
'First' has two meanings: priority in essence vs. priority in another aspect—confusing them leads to errors in definition.

In this passage, the author introduces the ranks of substances (first, second, third) and clarifies a crucial ambiguity: the term 'first' can indicate either priority in the very meaning of a concept or priority in a different respect. Understanding this distinction is essential for properly classifying substances in logic and ontology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
The passage is about substances (jawāhir) and their ranks (marātib). Notice that substances are classified as first, second, and third. Your task is to understand what determines these ranks.
The ranks correspond to the traditional classification of primary and secondary substances.
2
Recognize the two ways of being 'first'
The author explains that 'first' among things sharing a single nature (e.g., substance) can be understood in two ways: (1) first in that very meaning itself, like substance is first in existence relative to accident; (2) first in another respect, without being first or last in the essential meaning.
Example: Substance is prior to accident in existence—this is priority in the very meaning? Actually, the passage says substance is first in existence relative to accident, which is an instance of 'first in that very meaning'? Clarification: The author gives this as an example of the first sense: substance is first in existence relative to accident.
3
Apply to individual substances
The author states that individual substances (al-jawāhir al-shakhṣiyya) are not first in the reality of substantiality (ḥaqīqat al-jawhariyya). This means they are not first in the essential meaning of substance. They are first in another sense, likely ontological priority.
Individual substances are the primary existents, but the definition of substance is derived from the species or genus.
4
Understand the implications
This distinction prevents conflating ontological priority (existence) with definitional priority (essence). In Islamic logic, this informs debates on universals and particulars, especially in discussions of divine attributes and created beings.
For example, in kalam, the priority of essence over attributes is discussed using similar distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في مراتب الجواهر. وأما الجواهر فلها مراتب من حيث إنها هي الأولى والثانية والثالثة; وذلك لأن الأول في الأمور المشتركة في طبيعة واحدة قد يكون على وجهين: إما أن يكون أولا في ذلك المعنى بعينه كما أن الجوهر أول في الوجود بالقياس إلى العرض، وإما أن لا يكون أولا في ذلك المعنى ولا آخرًا أيضًا ولكن يكون أولا بوجه آخر، فالجواهر الشخصية ليست أولا في حقيقة الجوهرية
Section: On the ranks of substances. As for substances, they have ranks in terms of being first, second, and third. That is because the first among things sharing a single nature can be in two ways: either it is first in that very meaning, just as substance is first in existence in relation to accident, or it is not first in that meaning nor last either, but is first in another respect. So individual substances are not first in the reality of substantiality.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the ranks of substances. The author states that substances have ranks: first, second, and third. He then explains that the term 'first' when applied to things sharing a common nature can be understood in two ways. First, it can mean being first in that specific meaning itself, like substance is first in existence relative to accident (i.e., substance is prior to accident). Second, it can mean being first in another respect without being first or last in the essential meaning. The author then gives an example: individual substances are not first in the reality of substantiality; that is, they are not the most fundamental in terms of the definition of substance. This sets up the traditional distinction in Aristotelian logic between primary substance (individuals) and secondary substance (species and genera). Although the passage cuts off, it clearly indicates that individual substances are considered first in one sense (perhaps ontologically) but not in the definitional sense.

Distinguishing First from Primary: Ranks of Substances
Author explains three ranks of substances and distinguishes 'first' from 'primary'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish 'first' (awwal) from 'primary' (awla) and identify the three ranks of substances in terms of priority.

Core Idea
Not every primary thing is first: individuals are primary in existence but not in quiddity.

In this passage, the author explores the different ways something can be 'first' when substances share a common nature. He introduces a crucial distinction between being first in the very meaning (awwal) and being primary (awla) in some other respect—like existence or perfection. Understanding this distinction is key to correctly analyzing ontological hierarchies in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of 'first'
Look at the beginning: 'the first in matters sharing a single nature can be in two ways.' Find the two ways: either first in that very meaning (like substance is first in existence relative to accident) or first in another way (not in that meaning nor last).
This sets up the distinction between awwal and awla.
2
Understand why individuals are not 'first' in substantiality
The passage says individual substances are not first in the reality of substantiality (ḥaqīqa al-jawhariyya). Why? Because that reality belongs to the quiddity (universal), not to individuals. Individuals are, however, primary (awla) in substantiality due to their priority in existence, perfection, and being the subject of definition.
3
Grasp the difference between awwal and awla
The author explicitly states: 'There is a difference between first and primary—not everything that is primary in a thing is its first.' Analyze the examples: substance is first in existence relative to accident (awwal), but individuals are primary (awla) in substantiality without being first in the essential meaning.
This is the core logical distinction of the lesson.
4
Apply the distinction to the three ranks of substances
The passage mentions that substances have ranks of first, second, and third. From the context, the first rank is the universal quiddity (awwal in the very meaning), the second rank might be individuals (primary in existence), and the third could be something else (like universal substances as predicated). Reflect on how this ranking follows from the distinction.
The exact third rank is not fully detailed, but the framework is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواهر فلها مراتب من حيث إنها هي الأولى والثانية والثالثة؛ وذلك لأن الأول في الأمور المشتركة في طبيعة واحدة قد يكون على وجهين: إما أن يكون أولا في ذلك المعنى بعينه كما أن الجوهر أول في الوجود بالقياس إلى العرض، وإما أن لا يكون أولا في ذلك المعنى ولا آخرًا أيضًا ولكن يكون أولا بوجه آخر، فالجواهر الشخصية ليست أولا في حقيقة الجوهرية وإن كانت أولى بالجوهرية. وفرق بين الأول والأولى فليس كل ما هو أولى الشيء فهو أوله بل قد يكون أولى به إذا كان لواحق الشيء، وكمالاته يكون له أكثر مما لغيره أو أقدم له في الوجود بما لغيره والشخصيات ليست أولا في حقيقة الجوهرية؛ إذ تلك الحقيقة للماهية لكنها هي الأولى بالجوهرية لما أنها أول من جهة الوجود ومن جهة ما يكون باعتباره الجوهر جوهرا؛ وهو الحصول في الأعيان لا في موضوع / ومن جهة الكمال والفضيلة أيضًا. ومن جهة السبق إلى التسمية أما من جهة الوجود فإن الجواهر الكلية من حيث هي كلية بالفعل فهي إما مقولة بالقياس إلى الجزئيات، أو باعتبار النسبة إليها أو بأن وجودها ذلك أن يكون مقولة بوجه ما على موضوعات؛ فلا بد لها من الموضوعات والشخص لا يحتاج في أن يكون شخصا أي غير مقول معناه قولا وجوديًا أو وهميّا على كثرته إلى أن يكون شيء آخر مقولا عليه وعلى غیره وإلا لكان من شرط كل شخص أن يكون معه غيره. ولما كان كل شخص غنيا في تقرر وجوده عن صاحبه فهو غني عن الكلي، فإن قلت إن الكلي كما أنه كلي بالقياس إلى الجزئي كذلك الجزئي جزئي بالقياس إلى الكلي، وكما جزئي، كذلك أن ماهية الجزئي من حيث هي لا تتعلق بالكلي بل من حيث هو جزئي، ماهية الكلي لا تتعلق من حيث هي بل تتعلق من حيث هو هو كلي. فنقول لا كلام في الكلي والجزئي من حيث هما متضايفان بل يعني بالكلي ما يكون مقولا على كثيرين وبالجزئي ما لا يكون كذلك بل واحد بالعدد كزيد وعمرو، وإنه لا يقال الكلي مقابلة المضاف فلا يتعلق وجوده إذن بطبيعة الكلي. ثم لقائل أن يقول كما أن الشخص بعينه لا يتعلق وجوده بأن يكون الكلي موجودًا، فالكلي أيضًا لا يتعلق بالشخص بعينه. لكنا نقول المعنى بالشخص هو الطبيعة الشخصية على الإطلاق لا هو الشخص بعينه، ولا يتعلق لتلك
As for substances, they have ranks in terms of being first, second, and third. That is because the first in matters sharing a single nature can be in two ways: either it is first in that very meaning, as substance is first in existence relative to accident; or it is not first in that meaning nor last, but is first in another way. So individual substances are not first in the reality of substantiality, even if they are primary in substantiality. There is a difference between 'first' and 'primary'—not everything that is primary in a thing is its first; rather, it may be primary in it if the attachments and perfections of the thing belong to it more than to others, or it is prior in existence to what belongs to others. And the individuals are not first in the reality of substantiality, for that reality belongs to the quiddity; but they are primary in substantiality because they are first in terms of existence and in terms of that by which substance is considered substance—that is, obtaining in individuals not in a subject—and in terms of perfection and virtue, and in terms of precedence in naming. As for existence: universal substances insofar as they are actually universal are either predicated in relation to particulars, or by consideration of the relation to them, or by their existence being that they are predicated in some way on subjects; so they necessarily have subjects. The individual does not need, in order to be an individual—i.e., not meaning something predicated existentially or imaginally on its multiples—to have something else predicated on it and on others; otherwise, every individual would require another to exist. Since every individual is independent in the determination of its existence from its fellow, it is independent of the universal. If you say that just as the universal is universal in relation to the particular, so the particular is particular in relation to the universal, and just as the particular is particular, so the quiddity of the particular does not relate to the universal in itself but only insofar as it is particular, while the quiddity of the universal does not relate in itself but only insofar as it is universal. We say: there is no dispute concerning universal and particular as correlatives; rather, by 'universal' is meant that which is predicated of many, and by 'particular' that which is not so but is one in number, like Zayd and 'Amr; and it is not said that the universal is the correlative of the added, so its existence is not dependent on the nature of the universal. Then someone may say: just as the individual person per se does not have his existence dependent on the universal being existent, so the universal also does not depend on the individual per se. But we say: the meaning of 'individual' is the individual nature absolutely, not the individual per se, and it does not depend on that.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage classifies substances into three ranks: first, second, and third. The author begins by explaining that 'first' can be understood in two ways: first in the very meaning (like substance is first in existence relative to accident) or first in another way (not in the same meaning but still prior). Individual substances are not first in the reality of substantiality (ḥaqīqa al-jawhariyya)—that belongs to the universal quiddity—but they are primary (awla) in substantiality because they are prior in existence, in being the subject of the definition, and in perfection. The difference between 'first' (awwal) and 'primary' (awla) is crucial: not everything primary is the first. The author then elaborates: universal substances as universals require subjects because they are predicated of many, whereas individuals are self-sufficient and do not depend on universals for their existence. The relation between universal and particular is a correlative relation (taḍāyuf), but the particular individual is defined as one in number (like Zayd and 'Amr), not as a correlate. The passage ends with a dialectical exchange: an objector claims that just as the individual does not depend on the universal, the universal does not depend on the individual. The author responds that by 'individual' is meant the individual nature absolutely (al-ṭabī'a al-shakhṣiyya ʿalā al-iṭlāq), not a specific person per se, and that nature does not depend on any particular individual. This section is cut off but continues the argument.

Key Terms
الأول
الأولى
الشخص

Three Senses of Priority in Substances
Author enumerates three senses of priority in substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish priority by substantiality, by perfection, and by naming, and apply each to the case of individuals and universals.

Core Idea
Priority in substances has three distinct meanings: essential, perfective, and nominal.

The author introduces three ways a substance can be prior to another: by its nature as a subject (substantiality), by its degree of perfection, and by being first known (naming). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for avoiding equivocation in ontological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of priority
Locate in the passage the three distinct senses of priority mentioned: priority in substantiality, priority in perfection/virtue, and priority in naming.
The text explicitly labels them: 'bi i'tibar al-jawhariyya', 'bi al-kamal wa al-fadila', 'al-sabq ila al-tasmiya'.
2
For each type, state the criterion
Determine what makes something prior in that sense. Priority in substantiality: not needing a subject. Priority in perfection: being a principle or subject for others. Priority in naming: being first known to exist not in a subject.
3
Compare the three criteria
Notice that these criteria can conflict: a universal substance is prior in perfection but posterior in substantiality. The author warns against conflating them.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Consider the individual Zayd. Is he prior to the species 'human'? In substantiality, yes (as subject). In perfection, no (species is principle). In naming, yes (individuals first known).
This exercise prevents mixing the senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنطوق الطبيعة في الوجود بوجود الطبيعة الكلية من حيث هي كلية، وأما الطبيعة الكلية فهي متعلقة بشخص ما لا محالة. وبالجملة فالكلام في ماهية الجوهر من حيث هي كلية لا من حيث هي ماهية والمنطقي ينظر في الأشياء من حيث هي كلية . وأما التقدم باعتبار الجوهرية وهي أنها ماهية من شأنها إذا وجدت أن لا تحتاج إلى موضوع، والجواهر الأولى بهذه الصفة على خلاف الجواهر الكلية. وأما التقدم بالكمال والفضيلة فقد قال قوم: «إنها إذا كانت موضوعات وأصولا لغيرها كانت هي أفضل لكنه لا يتم إلا وأن يكون الأصل يجب أن يكون أفضل والموضوع كذلك، وفيه من الكلام بل ربما كان ذو الأصل الذي له الأصل وفضيلته أفضل من الأصل. وأما السبق إلى التسمية فلأن أول شيء عرف أنه موجود لا في موضوع فهو الأشخاص الجزئية، وبالحري أن يكون سابقة إذا كانت موضوعات لكلياتها على سبيل «على» والأعراض على سبيل «في»، فكان كل شيء وجوده إما أن يكون مقولا عليها أو موجودًا فيها. والجواهر الكلية وإن كانت ثانية فإن لها فيما بينها تفاوتا فالنوع منها أولى بالجوهرية من الجنس. ألا ترى أنك إذا سألت عن زيد «ما هو؟» فقلت «إنسان» كان أتم من جوابك أنه حيوان». وكما أن الشخص مقدم على النوع لما أنه موضوع الجنس وكذلك النوع من الجنس؛ فإن حال الجنس من حيث هو كلي من النوع الذي هو دونه كحال النوع من الشخص الذي دونه ولا يقال كيف يكون النوع كالشخص والنوع لا يكون نوعا إلا بالقياس إلى الجنس. اللهم إلا أن يكون المعنى من النوع النوع السافل فإن المعنى بالنوع فيما نحن بصدده من الكلام هو طبيعة النوع من حيث هو كلي فقط، والنظر في طبيعة النوع من حيث هو كلي غير النظر في طبيعة النوع من حيث هو طبيعة النوع أو من حيث هو النوع.
ثم لقائل أن يقول لو كان كما ذكرتم لكانت الجواهر العقلية متأخرة عن المحسوسات يجب أن يكون العقل بل الباري [ تعالى ] متأخرا عن الأشخاص المحسوسة. لكنا نقول أما الباري تعالى فإنه منزه عن الجوهرية وعما يكون داخلا فيها وأما الجواهر العقلية فالأنواع والأجناس وإن كانت جواهر عقلية فليس كل العقليات هي أنواع وأجناس بل في العقليات مفردات قائمة في ذاتها لا تتعلق بموضوع يقال عليه أو فيه . وهذه المفردات العقلية أولى بالجوهرية من كل شيء كما في المفردات الجسمانية أسباب وجودها مثلا. وأما المقايسة التي تقدم ذكرها فلم تكن بين المحسوسات وهذه الجواهر العقلية بل بين الشخصيات والكليات . وإن كان في الجواهر العقلية كثرة شخصيته تعمها نوعيته، ونوعيته تعمها جنسيته؛ فالمناسبة بينها هذه المناسبة. ونسبة أن يكون ذلك موجودًا في بعضها دون بعض ولما مر ذكر المقايسات التي تجري بين هذه الجواهر ذاتا فلا بد من التعرض للمقايسات التي تجري بينها عرضًا. فنقول إن الأشخاص الجزئية وإن تفاضلت في أمور فإنها من حيث هي أشخاص فلا تقدم لبعض ماهياتها على بعض. وكذلك حال نوعياتها فإنه ليس زيد أولى بأن يقال عليه طبيعة نوعه من عمرو مثلا بل ربما كان أولى ببعض الأعراض كما إذا كان أعلم منه. وكذلك الإنسان النوعي فإنه لا يكون أولى بالجوهرية النوعية وحمل الجنس عليه من الفرس. وإن كان باعتبار الشرف أولى. ولا جواهر بعد الجواهر الأولى في الحقيقة إلا أنواعها وأجناسها.
وأما الفصول فإنها من جهة يجري مجرى الأنواع ومن جهة أخرى، فإما أن يعنى بها الصور التي هي كالنطق؛ وهذه هي غير محموله على زيد وعمرو وإن كانت جواهر ولا مقايسة بينها وبين الأنواع والأشخاص في اعتبار العموم للخصوص بل باعتبار البساطة والتركيب، وهي جواهر صورية لها فيما بين
The spoken [mantiq] nature exists by the existence of the universal nature insofar as it is universal; but the universal nature is necessarily connected to some individual. In sum, the discourse is about the quiddity of substance insofar as it is universal, not insofar as it is quiddity, and the logician considers things insofar as they are universal. As for priority with respect to substantiality—namely that it is a quiddity such that when it exists it does not need a subject—the primary substances have this attribute in contrast to universal substances. As for priority with respect to perfection and virtue, some people said: 'If they are subjects and principles for others, then they are better, but [this] is not complete unless the principle must be better and the subject likewise; but there is discussion about this—indeed, perhaps the thing having the principle, which has the principle and its virtue, is better than the principle.' As for precedence in naming, because the first thing known to exist not in a subject is the particular individuals, and it is fitting that they be prior if they are subjects for their universals in the mode of 'upon' and for accidents in the mode of 'in', so everything that exists is either predicated of them or exists in them. Universal substances, though secondary, have differentiation among themselves: the species among them is more entitled to substantiality than the genus. Do you not see that if you ask about Zayd 'What is he?' and you say 'Human', it is more complete than your answer 'Animal'? And just as the individual is prior to the species because it is the subject of the genus, so also the species is prior to the genus; for the condition of the genus insofar as it is universal with respect to the species that is below it is like the condition of the species with respect to the individual that is below it, and one does not say how the species is like the individual while the species is only a species in relation to the genus — unless the meaning of 'species' is the lowest species, for the meaning of 'species' in our present discussion is only the nature of the species insofar as it is universal, and considering the nature of the species insofar as it is universal is different from considering the nature of the species insofar as it is the nature of the species or insofar as it is the species. Then an objector might say: If it were as you mentioned, then the intellectual substances would be posterior to the sensibles; the Intellect, indeed even the Exalted [God], must be posterior to sensible individuals. But we say: As for the Exalted, He is exalted above substantiality and what enters into it; as for the intellectual substances, the species and genera, though they are intellectual substances, not all intellectual things are species and genera—rather among the intellectual things there are individuals subsisting in themselves not attached to a subject predicated of them or existing in them. These intellectual individuals are more entitled to substantiality than everything, as in the bodily individuals the causes of their existence, for example. As for the comparison mentioned earlier, it was not between sensibles and these intellectual substances, but between individuals and universals. Even if in intellectual substances there is a multiplicity: their individuality is encompassed by their specific nature, and their specific nature by their generic nature, so the proportion between them is this proportion. And since we have mentioned the comparisons that run between these substances essentially, we must address the comparisons that run between them accidentally. So we say: Particular individuals, though they differ in matters, insofar as they are individuals have no priority of one quiddity over another. Similarly for their specific natures: Zayd is not more entitled to have the nature of his species predicated of him than Amr, for example; rather he might be more entitled in terms of some accidents, as when he is more knowledgeable. Likewise, the specific humanity is not more entitled to specific substantiality and predication of the genus than the horse, though it may be more entitled in terms of honor. And there are no substances after the primary substances in reality except their species and genera. As for the differentiae, they are in one respect like the species and in another respect: either what is meant by them are the forms like rationality—these are not predicated of Zayd and Amr even though they are substances—and there is no comparison between them and species and individuals in terms of generality and particularity, but in terms of simplicity and composition, and they are formal substances having among themselves...
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the different ways priority (taqaddum) applies to substances (jawhar). The author first distinguishes between primary substances (individuals) and universal substances (species and genera), clarifying that the logician considers things from the perspective of universality. Three types of priority are examined: (1) priority by substantiality (bi i'tibar al-jawhariyya), which belongs to primary substances because they do not need a subject; (2) priority by perfection/kamal, where there is a debate—some claim that principles are better, but the author notes that the thing possessing the principle might be better; (3) priority in naming (al-sabq ila al-tasmiya), since individual substances are first known as existing not in a subject. The author then explains that among universal substances, the species is more entitled to substantiality than the genus, using the example that 'human' is a more complete answer than 'animal' when asked about Zayd's quiddity. The relationship between individual, species, and genus is reciprocal: the individual is subject for the species and genus, and the species is subject for the genus. The passage then addresses a potential objection about intellectual substances (e.g., al-Bari, the Intellect) being posterior if the theory were applied to them. The response clarifies that al-Bari is beyond substantiality, and among intellectual substances, there are individuals (mufradat) that are prior to species and genera. Finally, the author discusses accidental priority: individuals as such have no essential priority over each other, but may differ by accidents like knowledge. Similarly, the human species is not essentially prior to the horse, only in honor. The passage concludes with a note on differentiae (fusul), which are formal substances like rationality, and are not predicated of individuals but are simpler than species and genera. Throughout, the author encourages precision in distinguishing the different senses of priority.

Key Terms
التقدم
الجوهر
الكمال
التسمية

Key Takeaway
Priority can be essential, perfective, or nominal—each applies differently to substances, and one must specify the sense.

Species vs. Genus: Which is More Entitled to Substantiality?
Author argues that species is prior to genus in substantiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify why the species is prior to the genus in substantiality, using the Zayd example.

Core Idea
The species is more entitled to substantiality than the genus because it gives a more complete answer to 'what is it?'.

After introducing the three senses of priority, the author delves into the hierarchy among universal substances. He argues that among them, the species is more entitled to substantiality than the genus, using the intuitive example that answering 'human' for Zayd is more complete than 'animal'. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Recall the hierarchy of substances
Identify the chain: individual → species → genus. The passage explains that just as the individual is subject for species, the species is subject for genus.
The author says: 'the condition of the genus with respect to the species is like the condition of the species with respect to the individual.'
2
Extract the argument for species' priority
The author gives the example: asking 'what is Zayd?' and answering 'human' is more complete than 'animal'. This shows that the species reveals more about the essence, hence it is more substantial.
The text says: 'the species among them is more entitled to substantiality than the genus. Do you not see that if you ask about Zayd...'
3
Understand why the species is not itself a subject for the genus in the same way
The species is a species only in relation to a higher genus. The 'lowest species' is meant—the one that does not itself function as a genus for any lower species.
The author clarifies that the discourse is about the nature of the species insofar as it is universal, not as a specific natural kind.

The spoken [mantiq] nature exists by the existence of the universal nature insofar as it is universal; but the universal nature is necessarily connected to some individual. In sum, the discourse is about the quiddity of substance insofar as it is universal, not insofar as it is quiddity, and the logician considers things insofar as they are universal. As for priority with respect to substantiality—namely that it is a quiddity such that when it exists it does not need a subject—the primary substances have this attribute in contrast to universal substances. As for priority with respect to perfection and virtue, some people said: 'If they are subjects and principles for others, then they are better, but [this] is not complete unless the principle must be better and the subject likewise; but there is discussion about this—indeed, perhaps the thing having the principle, which has the principle and its virtue, is better than the principle.' As for precedence in naming, because the first thing known to exist not in a subject is the particular individuals, and it is fitting that they be prior if they are subjects for their universals in the mode of 'upon' and for accidents in the mode of 'in', so everything that exists is either predicated of them or exists in them. Universal substances, though secondary, have differentiation among themselves: the species among them is more entitled to substantiality than the genus. Do you not see that if you ask about Zayd 'What is he?' and you say 'Human', it is more complete than your answer 'Animal'? And just as the individual is prior to the species because it is the subject of the genus, so also the species is prior to the genus; for the condition of the genus insofar as it is universal with respect to the species that is below it is like the condition of the species with respect to the individual that is below it, and one does not say how the species is like the individual while the species is only a species in relation to the genus — unless the meaning of 'species' is the lowest species, for the meaning of 'species' in our present discussion is only the nature of the species insofar as it is universal, and considering the nature of the species insofar as it is universal is different from considering the nature of the species insofar as it is the nature of the species or insofar as it is the species. Then an objector might say: If it were as you mentioned, then the intellectual substances would be posterior to the sensibles; the Intellect, indeed even the Exalted [God], must be posterior to sensible individuals. But we say: As for the Exalted, He is exalted above substantiality and what enters into it; as for the intellectual substances, the species and genera, though they are intellectual substances, not all intellectual things are species and genera—rather among the intellectual things there are individuals subsisting in themselves not attached to a subject predicated of them or existing in them. These intellectual individuals are more entitled to substantiality than everything, as in the bodily individuals the causes of their existence, for example. As for the comparison mentioned earlier, it was not between sensibles and these intellectual substances, but between individuals and universals. Even if in intellectual substances there is a multiplicity: their individuality is encompassed by their specific nature, and their specific nature by their generic nature, so the proportion between them is this proportion. And since we have mentioned the comparisons that run between these substances essentially, we must address the comparisons that run between them accidentally. So we say: Particular individuals, though they differ in matters, insofar as they are individuals have no priority of one quiddity over another. Similarly for their specific natures: Zayd is not more entitled to have the nature of his species predicated of him than Amr, for example; rather he might be more entitled in terms of some accidents, as when he is more knowledgeable. Likewise, the specific humanity is not more entitled to specific substantiality and predication of the genus than the horse, though it may be more entitled in terms of honor. And there are no substances after the primary substances in reality except their species and genera. As for the differentiae, they are in one respect like the species and in another respect: either what is meant by them are the forms like rationality—these are not predicated of Zayd and Amr even though they are substances—and there is no comparison between them and species and individuals in terms of generality and particularity, but in terms of simplicity and composition, and they are formal substances having among themselves...
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the different ways priority (taqaddum) applies to substances (jawhar). The author first distinguishes between primary substances (individuals) and universal substances (species and genera), clarifying that the logician considers things from the perspective of universality. Three types of priority are examined: (1) priority by substantiality (bi i'tibar al-jawhariyya), which belongs to primary substances because they do not need a subject; (2) priority by perfection/kamal, where there is a debate—some claim that principles are better, but the author notes that the thing possessing the principle might be better; (3) priority in naming (al-sabq ila al-tasmiya), since individual substances are first known as existing not in a subject. The author then explains that among universal substances, the species is more entitled to substantiality than the genus, using the example that 'human' is a more complete answer than 'animal' when asked about Zayd's quiddity. The relationship between individual, species, and genus is reciprocal: the individual is subject for the species and genus, and the species is subject for the genus. The passage then addresses a potential objection about intellectual substances (e.g., al-Bari, the Intellect) being posterior if the theory were applied to them. The response clarifies that al-Bari is beyond substantiality, and among intellectual substances, there are individuals (mufradat) that are prior to species and genera. Finally, the author discusses accidental priority: individuals as such have no essential priority over each other, but may differ by accidents like knowledge. Similarly, the human species is not essentially prior to the horse, only in honor. The passage concludes with a note on differentiae (fusul), which are formal substances like rationality, and are not predicated of individuals but are simpler than species and genera. Throughout, the author encourages precision in distinguishing the different senses of priority.
