Commentary
This passage examines the special properties (خواص) of substance (الجوهر). The author begins by noting two ways universal substances indicate 'any': they do not point to a specific individual, and they individuate substantially. This indicative property is exclusive to substances but not universal among them. Next, the author discusses the property of having no opposite for substances without a substratum, but qualifies that this is debated, as formal substances may have opposites. He then notes that quantity shares this property of having no opposite. Another property derived from the absence of opposites is that substance does not admit intensity and weakness, because intensity requires movement from one opposite to another. This holds for substances within the same species and degree, not across species. The author then addresses the most specific property: that an individual substance accepts opposites through its own change, unlike universals. However, this is not universal to all substances; only changeable, corporeal substances composed of matter and form accept opposites, excluding celestial bodies and purely formal substances. The property applies to both particulars and universals in a qualified sense. An objection about whether it applies to particulars like Zayd is answered: the property is primarily for the nature of corporeal substance, and universals are described by it insofar as their individuals accept opposites. Finally, the author distinguishes this from accidents like surface, which accept opposites only due to change in the underlying body. The true meaning is change in itself without mediation. This detailed analysis clarifies the scope of these properties.

أشد وأضعف

Key Takeaway
Substance has no opposite (qualifiedly) and thus does not admit degrees, a property shared with quantity.

Accepting Opposites Through Change: A Limited Property
Author enumerates and analyzes the special properties of substance and their scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which substances accept opposites through change and which do not, and explain the difference between substance and accident in this regard.

Core Idea
Only changeable corporeal substances accept opposites through change, not celestial or formal substances.

The final part of the passage discusses what might seem the most specific property of substance: that an individual substance can accept opposites (like hot and cold) through its own change. However, the author carefully limits this property to only some substances. This lesson unpacks that limitation and explores how this property differs from accidental change, using the example of a surface.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent most specific property
Find the phrase 'أخص الخواص بالجوهر هو أن ماهيته ماهية إذا تشخصت وضعت الأضداد'. The author states a common belief: that substance's quiddity, when individualized, posits opposites, meaning one and the same substance can receive opposite qualities by changing itself.
2
Recognize the limitation: not all substances
The author immediately says this does not cover every substance. Only changeable substances and corporeal substances composed of matter and form (هيولي وصورة) accept opposites. Celestial bodies and formal substances do not.
This is a crucial restriction: even among corporeal substances, only some (e.g., terrestrial elements, not celestial spheres).
3
Understand the application to universals and particulars
The author addresses an objection: this property seems to apply to universals (kulliyāt) but not particulars (juz'iyyāt) like Zayd. The response: the property primarily attaches to the nature of corporeal substance, and universals are described by it insofar as their individuals accept opposites. So when we say 'the universal man accepts opposites', it means 'any individual man accepts opposites'.
4
Distinguish from accidental change
The author contrasts this with accidents like surface, which also accept opposites (black/white), but only due to change in the underlying body, not in itself. True change in itself is the criterion: the substance changes without mediation of another entity.
This distinction helps define what 'change in itself' means for substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالجواهر الكلية تدل على أي بوجهين: أحدهما أنه لا يدل على هذا المشار إليه بعينه بل على أي واحد كان، والثاني أنها تفرد إفرادًا جوهريًا. فهذه الخاصة المنسوبة إلى الإشارة خاصة لا توجد إلا في الجواهر، وإن كانت لا توجد في جميع الجواهر. وللجوهر خاصة تعم جميع أنواعه لكن ليست للجوهر بالقياس إلى كل عرض بل بالقياس إلى البعض؛ وهي أنه لا ضد له إذا كان لا موضوع له لكنه في حيز المنع فإنه لا يبعد أن يكون للجواهر الصورية ضد. وليس على المنطقي أن يحاول إبانة هذه الأشياء بالتحقيق بل ليس في وسعه هذا. وأما الجسم الحار والبارد فإنهما وإن كانا لا يتضادان بذاتهما لكنهما يتضادان بالعرض وهو عرضية التضاد بين الحرارة والبرودة. وهذه الخاصة تشاركها فيها الكمية فإنه لا ضد لها، وإنه لا يشكل بالصغير والكبير والقلة والكثرة؛ إذ التضاد في حيز المنع والكمية فيها كذلك: وذلك يعرف من بعد. ومما يتبع هذه الخاصة هو أن الجوهر لا يقبل الأشد والأضعف؛ إذ الاشتداد والضعف لا يمكن بدون التضاد فلا يمكن أن يكون الشيء متوجهًا عن حالة الضعف إلى حالة القوة، وعن حالة القوة إلى حالة الضعف إلا وأن يكون متوجهًا عن الضد إلى الضد. وهذه الخاصة بحسب المقايسة التي من طبيعة واحدة وحد واحد لا بحسب المقايسة التي تجري بالأولى والأخرى، فليس شخص من الإنسان بأشد في جوهريته من شخص آخر إنسانًا كان ذلك [50] الشخص أو فرسًا. وعلى هذا فيما يمكن من الأنواع في درجة واحدة. والكم أيضًا يشارك الجوهر في هذه الخاصة لما نبينه من بعد. وقد يعتقد في ظاهر الأمر أن أخص الخواص بالجوهر هو أن ماهيته ماهية إذا تشخصت وضعت الأضداد، فالواحد منه بعينه يقبل الأضداد بتغيره في نفسه على خلاف الكلي فإنه لا يقبلها؛ فهذه الخاصة أيضًا لا يعم كل جوهر؛ إذ كل جوهر ليس يقابل الأضداد بل إنما تقبلها الجواهر المتغيرة والجواهر الجسمانية المركبة من هيولي وصورة، ولا كل هذه المركبات الجسمانية. فإن كثيرًا من الأجسام السماوية لا تقبل الأضداد؛ وإنما تقبل ذلك بعض الجواهر الجسمانية؛ وهذه الخاصة تساوي ذلك البعض، وإنها لا تكون مختصة بجزئيات ذلك البعض بل تكون لكلياته أيضًا. فإنه لا يحمل عليها أن الواحد منه بالعدد يقبل كذا وكذا؛ وإنه وإن لم يقبل ذلك كليته بكليته فإن كليته موصوفة بأن الواحد منه بالعدد يقبل ذلك. فإن قلت هذه الخاصة بحيث يصح للكليات لا يمكن أن يصح للجزئيات؛ فإن زيدًا ليس يقال عليه إن الواحد منه بالعدد يقبل الأضداد. فنقول هذه الخاصة في النظر الأول لطبيعة الجوهر الجسماني ومنظورًا إلى ماهيتها؛ فمنها كلي ومنها جزئي. فالكلي يلحق الطبيعة لحوقًا على الوجه الذي قيل في بعض الخواص إلى تخصص ولا يعم موضوعات المخصوص ولكن قد يظن أن من الأعراض ما يكون بهذا الطريق كالسطح مثلًا؛ فإن واحدًا منه بعينه يقبل السواد والبياض لكنه إنما يقبلهما لتغير مزاج الجسم أولًا: وذلك من جملة ما يتغير به السطح من ضدّ إلى ضد. ولأنا نعني بتغيره في نفسه أن تعاقب الأضداد عليه لا يكون بواسطة لغيره في شيء آخر بل تغيره في ذاته؛ وبهذا وقع التفصيل عنه وعن غيره كذلك يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
Universal substances indicate 'any' in two ways: first, they do not indicate this particular referenced thing but rather any one whatsoever; second, they individuate substantially. So this property attributed to indication is a special property found only in substances, though not found in all substances. Substance has a special property for all its species but not relative to every accident, but relative to some: that it has no opposite if it has no substratum, but this is in the realm of denial, for it is not improbable that formal substances have an opposite. The logician is not required to try to make these things clear with certainty; rather this is beyond his capacity. As for the hot and cold body, although they are not opposites in themselves, they are opposites accidentally, which is the accidental nature of the opposition between heat and cold. Quantity shares this property, for it has no opposite, and this is not problematic with small and large, few and many, for opposition is in the realm of denial, and quantity is similarly so; that will be known later. Among what follows this property is that substance does not accept intensity and weakness, for intensity and weakness cannot exist without opposition, so a thing cannot move from a state of weakness to a state of strength, or from strength to weakness, unless it moves from opposite to opposite. This property is according to comparison that is of one nature and one limit, not according to comparison that runs by the first and the other; thus no person of mankind is more intense in substantiality than another person, whether that person is human or a horse. And likewise for what is possible among species in one degree. Quantity also shares with substance this property, as we will explain later. It may be believed in the apparent view that the most specific property of substance is that its quiddity is a quiddity which, when individuated, posits opposites, so one and the same thing accepts opposites by its change in itself, unlike the universal which does not accept them. So this property does not cover every substance, for not every substance receives opposites; rather only changeable substances and corporeal substances composed of matter and form receive them, and not all of these corporeal composites. For many celestial bodies do not accept opposites; only some corporeal substances accept that. This property equals that portion, and it is not exclusive to the particulars of that portion but belongs also to its universals. For it is not predicated of them that one and the same individual accepts such-and-such; but even if its universal does not accept that as a universal, the universal is described that one and the same individual accepts that. If you say: This property, insofar as it holds for universals, cannot hold for particulars; for Zayd is not said of him that one and the same individual accepts opposites. We say: This property, in the first consideration, belongs to the nature of corporeal substance, looked at in terms of its quiddity; thus from it are universal and particular. The universal attaches to the nature in a manner that was said for some properties until specification, and does not cover the subjects of the specified. But it may be thought that among accidents there is what is in this way, like surface for example; for one and the same surface accepts blackness and whiteness, but it only accepts them due to the change of the body's temperament first: that is among what the surface changes from opposite to opposite. For we mean by its change in itself that the succession of opposites upon it does not occur through something else in another thing, but rather its change is in itself. By this the distinction is made between it and others. Thus it is known through reflection, God willing.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage examines the special properties (خواص) of substance (الجوهر). The author begins by noting two ways universal substances indicate 'any': they do not point to a specific individual, and they individuate substantially. This indicative property is exclusive to substances but not universal among them. Next, the author discusses the property of having no opposite for substances without a substratum, but qualifies that this is debated, as formal substances may have opposites. He then notes that quantity shares this property of having no opposite. Another property derived from the absence of opposites is that substance does not admit intensity and weakness, because intensity requires movement from one opposite to another. This holds for substances within the same species and degree, not across species. The author then addresses the most specific property: that an individual substance accepts opposites through its own change, unlike universals. However, this is not universal to all substances; only changeable, corporeal substances composed of matter and form accept opposites, excluding celestial bodies and purely formal substances. The property applies to both particulars and universals in a qualified sense. An objection about whether it applies to particulars like Zayd is answered: the property is primarily for the nature of corporeal substance, and universals are described by it insofar as their individuals accept opposites. Finally, the author distinguishes this from accidents like surface, which accept opposites only due to change in the underlying body. The true meaning is change in itself without mediation. This detailed analysis clarifies the scope of these properties.

ماهية
هيولي
صورة
تغير في نفسه

Why Definitions of Quantity Fail: Circularity
Author presents and refutes two definitions of quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a circular definition and explain why it fails as a proper logical definition.

Core Idea
A definition that uses a term defined by the definiendum is no definition at all.

In this passage, the author of the matn rejects two common definitions of quantity because they fall into circularity. The first defines quantity through equality, but equality itself cannot be defined without presupposing quantity. The second defines it through division, which similarly depends on quantity. This lesson teaches you to detect such circular definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
What term is being defined? Here, 'quantity' (al-kammiyya). The author is examining definitions of this category.
2
Extract the proposed definiens
The first definition: 'that by which something is capable of equality and inequality'. The second: 'that by which something is capable of division and non-division'. Write them down in Arabic or English.
3
Check for circular dependency
Does the definiens contain a term that itself requires the definiendum? For definition 1, 'equality' is a concept that only makes sense in relation to quantity. The author notes that equality is defined through 'union' (al-ittihad), which is a kind of quantity. For definition 2, 'division' is itself a quantitative notion.
The Arabic text cuts off but the pattern is clear: the definiens relies on terms that presuppose quantity.
4
Explain why the definition fails
A proper definition must clarify the unknown by the known. Here, the definiens uses terms that are either undefined or themselves require quantity to be understood. This makes the definition circular and therefore useless for real definition (hadd).
5
Generalize the rule
Any definition that implicitly uses the very thing being defined (or a synonym) is invalid. This applies not just to quantity but to any concept in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [في الكمية ] أما الكمية فإنها من جملة ما يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة [1] كما يقال إنها عبارة عما به يكون الشيء قابلا للمساواة واللامساواة؛ وإنه فاسد لما أنه لا يمكن تعريف المساواة إلا بالاتحاد [ب] أو يقال إنها عبارة عما به يكون الشيء قابلا للقسمة واللا قسمة؛ وإنه فاسد أيضا لما أن القابل للقسمة هو
Section on Quantity. As for quantity, it is among what is known by various definitions. It is said that it is an expression for that by which something is capable of equality and inequality. This is invalid because equality cannot be defined except by union [or unity?]. Alternatively, it is said that it is an expression for that by which something is capable of division and non-division. This is also invalid because that which is divisible is...
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage introduces the category of quantity (الكمية) and criticizes two proposed definitions. The author states that quantity is known through various definitions. The first definition: 'quantity is that by which something is capable of equality and inequality.' The author declares this invalid because equality itself cannot be defined except through union (الاتحاد), leading to circularity. The second definition: 'quantity is that by which something is capable of division and non-division.' This is also invalid because the notion of 'that which is divisible' itself presupposes quantity. The passage is cut off, but the pattern suggests a critique of defining a category by a property that itself belongs to the same category. This illustrates the logical principle that definitions must not be circular.

مساواة

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must not rely on a term that itself presupposes the definiendum.

Refining the Special Property of Substance: Scope and Predication
Author refines a special property of substance and clarifies its predication on universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scope of the special property 'receiving opposites' in substance and explain how it can be predicated of universals without applying to every particular.

Core Idea
A logical property can be true of a universal nature without being true of each particular instance.

In this passage, the author refines a special property of substance—receiving opposites—by specifying that it applies only to changing corporeal substances, not to all substances. He then addresses a subtle objection: if the property is universal, how can it fail to apply to particulars like Zayd? The response reveals an important logical distinction between properties of universals and properties of particulars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the special property (khassa) under discussion
Find the property that the author examines. It is qabwl al-addad (receiving opposites). This property is claimed to be a special property of some substances.
The author uses the term 'خاصة' (khassa) to refer to this property.
2
Determine which substances possess this property
The author states that not all substances receive opposites. Only changing (mutaghayyira) and corporeal substances composed of hyle and form (jismaniyya murakkaba min hayula wa sura) do. Even among those, some heavenly bodies do not. So the property applies only to a subset: changing corporeal substances.
3
Understand the objection (mu'tarid)
The objection: if the property is co-extensive with that subset, why can't it be directly predicated of each particular? For instance, can we say 'Zayd (as a particular) receives opposites'? The objector says no, because Zayd as one individual does not undergo opposite changes in the same way.
The objection hinges on the difference between universal and particular predication.
4
Follow the author's response (jawab)
The author distinguishes two perspectives: when considering the nature (tabi'a) of corporeal substance as a universal (kulli), the property attaches to that universal nature. It does not require that every particular individual be described by it in the same manner. The example of a surface (sath) receiving black and white is used: the change is intrinsic to the surface itself, not mediated by another subject.
5
Apply the distinction to Islamic logic
This shows that in Islamic logic, a special property can be true of a kind without being directly predicable of each individual in the same way. This is important for definitions and arguments about universals.
Example: The property 'rational' is true of the universal 'human', but each individual human is not 'rational' in the same sense as the universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه لا يقبلها؛ فهذه الخاصة أيضًا لا يعم كل جوهر؛ إذ كل جوهر ليس يقابل الأضداد بل إنما تقبلها الجواهر المتغيرة والجواهر الجسمانية المركبة من هيولي وصورة، ولا كل هذه المركبات الجسمانية. فإن كثيرًا من الأجسام السماوية لا تقبل الأضداد؛ وإنما تقبل ذلك بعض الجواهر الجسمانية؛ وهذه الخاصة تساوي ذلك البعض، وإنها لا تكون مختصة بجزئيات ذلك البعض بل تكون لكلياته أيضًا. فإنه لا يحمل عليها أن الواحد منه بالعدد يقبل كذا وكذا؛ وإنه وإن لم يقبل ذلك كليته بكليته فإن كليته موصوفة بأن الواحد منه بالعدد يقبل ذلك. فإن قلت هذه الخاصة بحيث يصح للكليات لا يمكن أن يصح للجزئيات؛ فإن زيدًا ليس يقال عليه إن الواحد منه بالعدد يقبل الأضداد. فنقول هذه الخاصة في النظر الأول لطبيعة الجوهر الجسماني ومنظورًا إلى ماهيتها؛ فمنها كلي ومنها جزئي. فالكلي يلحق الطبيعة لحوقاً على الوجه الذي قيل في بعض الخواص إلى تخصص ولا يعم موضوعات المخصوص ولكن قد يظن أن من الأعراض ما يكون بهذا الطريق كالسطح مثلا؛ فإن واحدًا منه بعينه يقبل السواد والبياض لكنه إنما يقبلهما لتغير مزاج الجسم أولاً: وذلك من جملة ما يتغير به السطح من ضدّ إلى ضد. ولأنا نعني بتغيره في نفسه أن تعاقب الأضداد عليه لا يكون بواسطة لغيره في شيء آخر بل تغيره في ذاته؛ وبهذا وقع التفصيل عنه وعن غيره كذلك يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
For it does not accept them; so this special property also does not apply to every substance, because not every substance receives opposites; rather only changing substances and corporeal substances composed of hyle and form receive them, and not all of these composite corporeal substances. For many of the heavenly bodies do not receive opposites; rather some corporeal substances receive them; and this special property equals that part, and it is not restricted to the particulars of that part but belongs also to its universals. For it is not predicated of them that the one of them by number receives such-and-such; and although it is not accepted by its universality as a whole, its universality is described as that the one of it by number receives that. If you say: this special property, being valid for universals, cannot be valid for particulars; for Zayd is not said that the one of him by number receives opposites. We say: this special property, in the first consideration, belongs to the nature of corporeal substance and considered as to its quiddity; some of it is universal and some particular. The universal attaches to the nature in the manner that was said concerning some special properties until specification, and does not apply to the subjects of the specified, but it might be thought that some accidents are like that, e.g., surface; for one and the same surface receives blackness and whiteness, but it accepts them only due to the change of the body's temperament first; and that is among the things by which the surface changes from opposite to opposite. And because we mean by its change in itself that the succession of opposites upon it is not through another in something else, rather its change is in its essence; and by this the distinction between it and others occurs; thus it is known by reflection, if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refinement of a special property (khassa) of substance. The author begins by noting that the property of receiving opposites does not apply to every substance because many substances (e.g., heavenly bodies) do not change. It applies only to some corporeal substances. The author then addresses an objection: if the property is predicated of universals (e.g., 'this kind of substance receives opposites'), it cannot be predicated of particulars individually (e.g., 'Zayd receives opposites'). The response distinguishes two perspectives: when considering the nature of corporeal substance itself, the property attaches to the universal nature, not necessarily to every particular. The author uses the example of a surface that receives blackness and whiteness due to the body's change, emphasizing that the change is intrinsic to the surface itself. This clarifies that logical predication can hold at the universal level without applying identically to every particular instance.

Key Terms
خاصة (khassa)
جوهر (jawhar)
الأضداد (al-addad)
كلّي (kulli)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the special property of receiving opposites does not apply to all substances?
A) Because all substances are immutable.
B) Because it applies only to changing corporeal substances.
C) Because it is a universal property.
D) Because it is accidental.

Key Takeaway
A special property (khassa) can be co-extensive with a part of the genus and true of the universal nature without being directly predicated of each particular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the argument 'Every substance receives opposites. The world is a substance. Therefore the world receives opposites.' Using the author's refinement, explain why the major premise is false for all substances and how it should be modified.
?
Consider the special property 'causes intoxication' in Fiqh. Does it apply to the universal 'khamr' (wine) or to each particular instance? Use the author's distinction between universal and particular predication to frame your answer.

Defining Quantity: The Correct Logical Definition
Author defines quantity and rejects two alternative definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct definition of quantity and explain why two alternative definitions are invalid.

Core Idea
Quantity is that in which something counts another, not equality or division.

This passage teaches us how to evaluate definitions critically. The author presents three definitions of quantity and rejects two before settling on the correct one. This method is essential for logicians defining any category.

Guided Path
1
List the three definitions
The author mentions a definition using equality, one using division, and his own using counting.
2
Analyze the objection to equality
Equality cannot be defined without circular reference to quantity itself.
Circularity is a common logical error.
3
Analyze the objection to division
Division only applies to continuous quantities, so it is too narrow.
This definition fails to include discrete numbers.
4
Understand the positive definition
Quantity is that in which something can exist that counts another, either potentially (magnitudes) or actually (numbers).
This definition covers both continuous and discrete.
5
Distinguish continuous vs discrete
Continuous quantity allows for potential division; discrete quantity is counted as units.
This distinction is crucial in later discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [في الكمية ] أما الكمية فإنها من جملة ما يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة [1] كما يقال إنها عبارة عما به يكون الشيء قابلا للمساواة واللامساواة؛ وإنه فاسد لما أنه لا يمكن تعريف المساواة إلا بالاتحاد [ب] أو يقال إنها عبارة عما به يكون الشيء قابلا للقسمة واللا قسمة؛ وإنه فاسد أيضًا لما أن القابل للقسمة هو الكمية المتصلة؛ بل الأولى أن يقال إنها عبارة عما يمكن أن يوجد فيه ما يعد غيره سواء كان ذلك بالقوة كما في المقادير، أو بالفعل كما في الأعداد. وإنها أي الكمية تذكر عقب الجوهر لمعان مستدعيه : منها المشاركة في الخواص؛ والمشاركة مما تقدم ذكره من قبل، ومنها أن الكمية أعم وجودًا من الكيفية وأصح وجودًا من المضاف. أما أنه أعم فلأن العدد من الكمية؛ وليس مقصورًا [۱] / في وجوده على الأمور المقارنة للحركة والمادة دون المفارقة التي لا تقبل كيفية، ولا شيئًا خارجًا عن جوهرها . وأما أنها أصح فلأن المضاف غير متقرر في ذات موضوعه تقرر الكمية، وأيضًا فإن الكمية المتصلة توجد في جميع الأجسام الطبيعية من غير اختلاف، والكيفيات مختلفة فيها. والكمية إذا شاركت الكيفية في الجواهر فإنها تلزم الأول منها وهو الجسم، والكيفيات تلزم الجواهر النوعية السافلة والمتوسطة بعد الجسمية. ويمكن أن يقال من الوجوه ما عداها لكن الاشتغال بذلك اشتغال بما لا يغنيه؛ ولا يتم من المباحث فيها هي أنها جوهر أو عرض فإنها إذا كانت عبارة عن الجسمية التي تقارن المادة فبالحري أن تكون صورة مقومة. والصورة المقومة للجواهر جوهر لكن لا على المنطقي أن يتعرض لمثل هذا، أو يبينه على سبيل التحقيق؛ وقد كان بيانه في صناعة أخرى لكن يلزمه أن يقول إن الجسم لا يكون إلا و أن يكون متناهيا لكن حد الجسم من حيث هو جسم غير حد الجسم المتناهي. ولذلك قد يعقل الجسم دون التناهي ما لم يبرهن ببرهان يقرره؛ فالتناهي لا يكون داخلا في ماهية الجسم فالسطحي ليس جزءًا لحده. ومع ذلك فإنه وإن كان كل جسم متناهيًا فالأبعاد ليس بواجب حصولها في الجسم بالفعل فإن الكرة من حيث هي كرة جسم وليس تفرض فيها أبعاد متميزة بل الجسم إنما هو جسم؛ لأنه في طباعه بحيث يمكن أن تفرض فيه
Section on Quantity. As for quantity, it is among things defined by different definitions: as it is said that it is an expression for that by which a thing becomes capable of equality and inequality; but this is invalid because equality cannot be defined except by union. Or it is said that it is an expression for that by which a thing becomes capable of division and indivisibility; this is also invalid because that which is capable of division is continuous quantity. Rather the better is to say that it is an expression for that in which can exist something that counts another, whether potentially, as in magnitudes, or actually, as in numbers. And it — i.e., quantity — is mentioned after substance due to aspects requiring that: among them, participation in properties; and the participation was mentioned previously, and among them that quantity is more general in existence than quality and more established in existence than relation. As for its being more general, because number is a quantity and is not confined in its existence to things associated with motion and matter, excluding separate things that do not accept quality nor anything outside their essence. And as for its being more established, because relation is not settled in the essence of its subject as quantity is settled, and also because continuous quantity exists in all natural bodies without difference, while qualities differ within them. And quantity, when it shares with quality in substances, it accompanies the first of them, which is body, while qualities accompany the lower and intermediate specific substances after corporeality. And it can be said from other aspects, but preoccupation with that is preoccupation with what does not benefit; and the investigation of whether it is substance or accident is not completed. For if it is an expression for corporeality which is associated with matter, then it is likely to be a constitutive form. And the constitutive form of substances is a substance, but it is not for the logician to discuss such a thing or to explain it in a verificatory manner; its explanation was in another discipline, but it is necessary for him to say that body is only finite, but the definition of body as such is not the definition of the finite body. Therefore body may be conceived without finitude unless proven by a demonstration that settles it; so finitude is not included in the essence of body, thus surface is not part of its definition. Nevertheless, even though every body is finite, dimensions are not necessarily actually present in body; for the sphere as such is a body, and one does not posit in it distinct dimensions, rather body is only body because in its nature it is such that dimensions can be posited in it...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a definitional discussion of quantity (al-kamiyya). The author begins by presenting two common definitions and rejecting them. The first definition — 'that by which a thing is capable of equality and inequality' — is invalid because equality cannot be defined without circularity. The second — 'that by which a thing is capable of division and indivisibility' — is also invalid because that which is divisible is only continuous quantity. The author then proposes a better definition: quantity is that in which something can exist that counts another, either potentially (as in magnitudes) or actually (as in numbers). Next, the author explains why quantity is discussed after substance: it participates in some properties of substance, it is more general in existence than quality (since number exists even in immaterial things that lack quality), and it is more established than relation (since relation is not as inherent in its subject as quantity). The author also notes that quantity accompanies the first substance, which is body, while qualities accompany lower specific substances. The discussion then turns to whether quantity is a substance or accident, but the author states that such an investigation belongs to another discipline, not logic. However, the logician must note that body is always finite, but finitude is not part of the essence of body as such; it can be conceived without finitude. Dimensions are not necessarily actual in body; a sphere is a body without distinct dimensions being posited already. This clarifies the logical definition of body and quantity.

Key Terms
كمية
مساواة
قسمة
متصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the definition of quantity as 'that by which a thing is capable of equality and inequality'?
A) Because equality is not a property of quantity.
B) Because equality cannot be defined except by quantity, leading to circularity.
C) Because inequality is not relevant to quantity.
D) Because all quantities are equal.

Key Takeaway
Quantity is defined by its capacity to have something that counts another, not by equality or division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the author's method, evaluate the definition of 'wudu' (ablution) as 'washing of limbs'. Is it complete? What might it miss?
?
Construct a definition for the category 'khabar' (report) in hadith sciences, ensuring it includes both mutawatir and ahad.

Why Quantity Follows Substance and the Nature of Body
Author explains why quantity is placed after substance and discusses finitude of body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why quantity is placed after substance in the categories and why finitude is not part of the essence of body.

Core Idea
Quantity follows substance because it is more general than quality and more inherent than relation.

This passage explains the ordering of categories. Quantity is placed after substance due to its properties relative to quality and relation. The author also clarifies that although all bodies are finite, finitude is not part of the essence of body.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reasons for ordering
The author gives three reasons: participation in properties, more general existence than quality, and more established existence than relation.
2
Understand generality
Number is a quantity that exists in immaterial things lacking quality, so quantity is more general.
Quality requires matter; quantity does not.
3
Understand establishment
Relation is less settled in its subject than quantity; quantity is inherent.
Also, continuous quantity exists uniformly in all natural bodies.
4
Examine the relation to body
Quantity accompanies the first substance, body, while qualities come later.
This supports the ordering.
5
Analyze finitude of body
Although every body is finite, finitude is not part of the essence of body; it can be conceived without finitude.
This is a logical distinction between essential and accidental properties.
